8 min
Gift of Debt Does Not Fulfill an Oath to Repay
Ruling on gift of debt not fulfilling oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether gift of debt fulfills an oath to repay and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Gift of debt is not repayment; the debtor's oath is not fulfilled.

When someone swears to repay a debt, and the creditor forgives the debt (makes it a gift), does that fulfill the oath? The Hanafi school clarifies that gift is not repayment because repayment is an act of the debtor, while gift is an act of the creditor. This lesson explores the ruling and the difference of opinion with Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act of gifting the debt by the creditor after the debtor swore to repay. The ruling: gift does not constitute qadha (repayment).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The reasoning (talil) is given: qadha is an act of the debtor, gift is an act of the creditor (dropping the debt). So the oath is not fulfilled.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf differs: he holds that the oath can be fulfilled because he does not require the conceptualization of discharge at the time of obligation. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require it.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that gift does not fulfill the oath, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الهبة أي ليس هبة الدين قضاء للدين، فلو حلف ليقضين دينه فهبة الدائن الدين لا يكون قضاء؛ لأن القضاء فعل المديون والهبة إسقاط للدين من الطالب فلا تتحقق المقاصة، فتبطل اليمين إذا كانت مؤقتة بإبرائه قبل الوقت؛ لأن القضاء لا يتصور بعد الإبراء، وفيه خلاف أبي يوسف بناء على أصله أن تصوّر البر وقت وجوبه ليس بشرط عنده وعندهما شرط على ما مر. (رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٣/١) (فتح)
لم يحنث حتى يقبض إلخ: أي بتفريق اختياري بأن قبض بعضه في أول النهار وبعضه في آخره؛ لأن شرط حنثه قبض الكل بوصف التفريق؛ لأنه أضاف القبض إلى دين معرف بالإضافة إلى الدائن فيتناول كله، فما دام عند المديون شيء من دينه باقياً لم يحنث؛ لعدم قبض الكل، وهو الشرط. ولو قيد باليوم بأن قال: لا يقبض دينه درهماً دون درهم اليوم، فقبض البعض في اليوم متفرقاً أو لم يقبض منه شيئاً لم يحنث؛ لأن شرط الحنث أخذ الكل في اليوم متفرقاً ولم يوجد. ولو قال: إن قبضت من ديني درهماً دون درهم حنث، والفرق أن شرط الحنث هنا قبض البعض متفرقاً وفي الأول قبض الكل بصفة التفريق، إذا أخذ الكل مجتمعاً أو قبض البعض متفرقاً لم يحنث لانعدام شرط الحنث. (رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٤/١) (فتح)
Gift (hibah) is not qadha: meaning gifting the debt does not constitute qadha of the debt. If he swears to repay his debt, then the creditor's gift of the debt is not qadha; because qadha is an act of the debtor, and gift is a dropping of the debt by the claimant, so set-off does not occur. So the oath becomes void if it was time-bound by the discharge before the time; because qadha cannot be imagined after discharge. There is a difference of opinion with Abu Yusuf based on his principle that the conceptualization of discharge at the time of obligation is not a condition for him, but for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) it is a condition, as has passed. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/373) (Fath)
He does not break the oath until he takes possession of all: meaning by optional division (tafriq ikhtiyari) such that he takes part at the beginning of the day and part at the end; because the condition for breaking the oath is taking possession of all with the description of division; because he added possession to the debt defined by addition to the creditor, so it includes all. So as long as something of his debt remains with the debtor, he does not break the oath due to lack of full possession, which is the condition. If he restricts with the day by saying: 'He does not take possession of his debt, a dirham without a dirham today,' then he takes possession of some during the day in parts or does not take possession of anything, he does not break the oath; because the condition of breaking is taking all in the day in parts, and it did not occur. If he says: 'If I take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham,' he breaks the oath. The difference is that the condition of breaking here is taking part in parts, while in the first it is taking all with the description of division. If he takes all together or takes part in parts, he does not break the oath due to the non-existence of the condition of breaking. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/374) (Fath)
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage combines two distinct but related rulings. First, the ruling on gifting the debt: if the creditor forgives the debt (hibah), that does not constitute qadha (payment) by the debtor. The oath to repay is not fulfilled; in fact, if the oath was time-bound, it becomes void because payment is no longer possible. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the oath is still fulfilled because in his view, the conceptualization of discharge at the due time is not a condition, but Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require that payment be possible at the time it is due. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that gift does not fulfill the oath. Secondly, the ruling on partial payment: if someone makes an oath about not repaying a debt 'dirham by dirham' (i.e., not paying in installments), then paying in parts does not break the oath unless all is paid. The precise wording matters: if the oath is 'I will not pay my debt dirham by dirham today,' then paying any part (even one dirham) in parts breaks the oath? Actually, the text distinguishes between two formulations. For example, if one says: 'I will not take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham (i.e., in parts),' then breaking the oath requires taking possession of all in a divided manner. If one takes all at once or takes part in a divided manner, the oath is not broken. Conversely, if one says: 'If I take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham,' then taking any part in parts breaks the oath. The key is the condition set in the oath formulation.

Key Terms
هبة الدين (hibat al-dayn): The creditor's forgiveness of the debt as a gift, extinguishing the debt without payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears to repay his debt to his friend by the end of the month. Before the end of the month, the friend says, 'I forgive your debt.' According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is correct?
A) The oath is fulfilled because the debt is no longer owed.
B) The oath is not fulfilled because forgiveness is not repayment.
C) The oath is fulfilled only if the debtor also makes a payment.
D) The oath becomes void and the debtor must pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Gift of debt does not fulfill an oath to repay because qadha requires the debtor's action.

Partial Payment and Oath Formulations
Ruling on partial payment and oath formulations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on partial payment to determine whether an oath is broken, based on the precise wording of the oath.

Core Idea
The wording of the oath determines whether partial payment breaks it; 'dirham without dirham' can require either all or part.

When someone swears about paying a debt in parts, the exact phrasing matters. This lesson examines two distinct formulations and how each affects the condition for breaking the oath. Understanding this prevents incorrect rulings in cases of installment payments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath formulations
First formulation: 'He does not take possession of his debt, a dirham without a dirham' (i.e., in parts). Second: 'If I take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham.'
2
State the condition for breaking the oath in the first formulation
The condition is taking possession of ALL the debt in a divided manner. If he takes all at once or takes part in parts, he does not break the oath.
3
State the condition for breaking the oath in the second formulation
The condition is taking possession of ANY part in a divided manner. If he takes any part in parts, he breaks the oath.
4
Analyze the difference
In the first, the negation applies to the entire debt; in the second, the affirmation applies to any part.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a specific oath wording, determine if partial payment breaks the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الهبة أي ليس هبة الدين قضاء للدين، فلو حلف ليقضين دينه فهبة الدائن الدين لا يكون قضاء؛ لأن القضاء فعل المديون والهبة إسقاط للدين من الطالب فلا تتحقق المقاصة، فتبطل اليمين إذا كانت مؤقتة بإبرائه قبل الوقت؛ لأن القضاء لا يتصور بعد الإبراء، وفيه خلاف أبي يوسف بناء على أصله أن تصوّر البر وقت وجوبه ليس بشرط عنده وعندهما شرط على ما مر. (رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٣/١) (فتح)
لم يحنث حتى يقبض إلخ: أي بتفريق اختياري بأن قبض بعضه في أول النهار وبعضه في آخره؛ لأن شرط حنثه قبض الكل بوصف التفريق؛ لأنه أضاف القبض إلى دين معرف بالإضافة إلى الدائن فيتناول كله، فما دام عند المديون شيء من دينه باقياً لم يحنث؛ لعدم قبض الكل، وهو الشرط. ولو قيد باليوم بأن قال: لا يقبض دينه درهماً دون درهم اليوم، فقبض البعض في اليوم متفرقاً أو لم يقبض منه شيئاً لم يحنث؛ لأن شرط الحنث أخذ الكل في اليوم متفرقاً ولم يوجد. ولو قال: إن قبضت من ديني درهماً دون درهم حنث، والفرق أن شرط الحنث هنا قبض البعض متفرقاً وفي الأول قبض الكل بصفة التفريق، إذا أخذ الكل مجتمعاً أو قبض البعض متفرقاً لم يحنث لانعدام شرط الحنث. (رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٤/١) (فتح)
Gift (hibah) is not qadha: meaning gifting the debt does not constitute qadha of the debt. If he swears to repay his debt, then the creditor's gift of the debt is not qadha; because qadha is an act of the debtor, and gift is a dropping of the debt by the claimant, so set-off does not occur. So the oath becomes void if it was time-bound by the discharge before the time; because qadha cannot be imagined after discharge. There is a difference of opinion with Abu Yusuf based on his principle that the conceptualization of discharge at the time of obligation is not a condition for him, but for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) it is a condition, as has passed. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/373) (Fath)
He does not break the oath until he takes possession of all: meaning by optional division (tafriq ikhtiyari) such that he takes part at the beginning of the day and part at the end; because the condition for breaking the oath is taking possession of all with the description of division; because he added possession to the debt defined by addition to the creditor, so it includes all. So as long as something of his debt remains with the debtor, he does not break the oath due to lack of full possession, which is the condition. If he restricts with the day by saying: 'He does not take possession of his debt, a dirham without a dirham today,' then he takes possession of some during the day in parts or does not take possession of anything, he does not break the oath; because the condition of breaking is taking all in the day in parts, and it did not occur. If he says: 'If I take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham,' he breaks the oath. The difference is that the condition of breaking here is taking part in parts, while in the first it is taking all with the description of division. If he takes all together or takes part in parts, he does not break the oath due to the non-existence of the condition of breaking. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/374) (Fath)
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage combines two distinct but related rulings. First, the ruling on gifting the debt: if the creditor forgives the debt (hibah), that does not constitute qadha (payment) by the debtor. The oath to repay is not fulfilled; in fact, if the oath was time-bound, it becomes void because payment is no longer possible. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the oath is still fulfilled because in his view, the conceptualization of discharge at the due time is not a condition, but Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require that payment be possible at the time it is due. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that gift does not fulfill the oath. Secondly, the ruling on partial payment: if someone makes an oath about not repaying a debt 'dirham by dirham' (i.e., not paying in installments), then paying in parts does not break the oath unless all is paid. The precise wording matters: if the oath is 'I will not pay my debt dirham by dirham today,' then paying any part (even one dirham) in parts breaks the oath? Actually, the text distinguishes between two formulations. For example, if one says: 'I will not take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham (i.e., in parts),' then breaking the oath requires taking possession of all in a divided manner. If one takes all at once or takes part in a divided manner, the oath is not broken. Conversely, if one says: 'If I take possession of my debt, a dirham without a dirham,' then taking any part in parts breaks the oath. The key is the condition set in the oath formulation.

Key Terms
تفريق اختياري (tafriq ikhtiyari): Voluntary division of payments, e.g., paying part in the morning and part in the afternoon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears: 'I will not take my debt dirham without dirham (i.e., in parts).' He receives 75 dirhams in the morning and 25 in the afternoon. According to the Hanafi school, has he broken his oath?
A) Yes, because he took the debt in installments.
B) No, because he did not take all at once.
C) Yes, because he took the entire debt in a divided manner.
D) No, because the oath was about not taking possession, and he did take possession.

Key Takeaway
In oaths about payment in parts, the key is whether the entire debt is taken in parts or only part of it.

Ruling on Oaths with Exceptions (Istithna')
Ruling on oaths with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths containing an exception (istithna') and explain why owning the excepted amount does not break the oath, as well as identify the types of property that count in such oaths.

Core Idea
In an oath with an exception, the speaker is understood to deny having more than the excepted amount.

This lesson examines the Hanafi interpretation of oaths that include an exception, such as 'I have no property except 100 dirhams.' Based on customary usage (urf), the scholars rule that such an oath negates only property above the excepted amount. Therefore, owning the excepted amount or less does not break the oath. We also explore what counts as property (mal) for the oath's violation.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The oath is not broken by owning the excepted amount or any lesser amount.
This is the core fiqh outcome.
2
Understand the urf-based interpretation
Custom interprets 'I have no property except X' as 'I have no more than X'.
The exception is understood as limiting the negation.
3
Identify the condition on extra property
For the oath to be broken, the extra property must be of a type that is zakatable (dinars, trade goods, grazing livestock).
Non-trade assets like personal houses are not considered 'mal' here.
4
Apply to examples
If the extra is non-trade assets, the oath remains unbroken even if their value exceeds the exception.
This shows the practical scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كان لي مال إلا مائة أو غير أو سوى فكذا، لم يحنث بملكها أو بعضها. أي من قال: 'إن كان لي مال إلا مائة درهم أو غير مائة أو سوى مائة فعبدي حر فلم يملك إلا مائة أو خمسين، لم يحنث؛ لأن المراد في العرف نفي ما زاد على المائة؛ لأن استثناء المائة استثناؤها بجميع أجزائها، فشرط حنثه ملك الزيادة على المائة ولم يوجد. و'غير' و'سوى' أداة الاستثناء مثل 'إلا'. ويشترط في الزيادة أن تكون من جنس مال الزكاة كالدنانير وعروض التجارة والسوائم. ولو كان له عروض وضياع ودور لغير التجارة زائداً على المائة لم يحنث. (فتح، مستخلص، عيني)
If I have property except 100 dirhams, or other than, or besides, then so, he does not break the oath by owning it or some of it. Meaning: someone who says, 'If I have property except 100 dirhams, or other than 100, or besides 100, then my slave is free,' and he owns only 100 or fifty, he does not break the oath; because the intended meaning in custom (urf) is the negation of what is over 100; because excepting 100 is excepting all its parts, so the condition for breaking his oath is owning extra above 100, and it did not occur. And 'ghayr' and 'siwa' are tools of exception like 'illa'. And it is required that the extra be of the type of zakat property, like dinars, trade goods, and grazing livestock. If he has non-trade goods, estates, and houses exceeding 100, he does not break the oath. (Fath, Mustakhlas, `Ayni)
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage covers oaths that include an exception, such as 'I have no property except 100 dirhams.' The Hanafi ruling is that such an oath is interpreted according to custom: the speaker intends to deny having more than 100 dirhams. Therefore, if he owns exactly 100 or less, the oath is not broken. The words 'ghayr' and 'siwa' are treated as exceptions like 'illa'. Additionally, the extra property (above the exception) must be of a type that is zakatable (like gold, silver, trade goods, livestock) to count as 'mal' (property) for the oath. Non-trade assets like personal houses and land (not for trade) are not considered 'mal' in this context, so they do not cause the oath to be broken even if their value exceeds 100.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception: excluding a specific amount from a general negation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I have no property except 1000 dirhams.' He actually owns exactly 1000 dirhams. Does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because he has property.
B) No, because the exception is interpreted as limiting the negation to more than 1000.
C) It depends on the kind of property.
D) Yes, unless he has no other assets.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath with an exception like 'except 100' is customarily interpreted as 'no more than 100', so owning the excepted amount does not break the oath, provided any extra property is of zakatable type.

Oath of Abstinence vs. Oath of Commission
Two complementary rulings on oaths with linguistic justification and a dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for oaths of abstinence and commission, derive them from the linguistic principle of indefinite in negation vs. affirmation, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on timing.

Core Idea
An indefinite in negation is general; an indefinite in affirmation is specific — this determines oath fulfillment.

When someone swears not to do something, how long must they abstain? And if they swear to do something, how many times must they perform it? The Hanafi school answers both questions through a single linguistic rule: the indefinite noun in a negative sentence implies all instances, while in an affirmative sentence it implies a single instance. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application to oaths, including a notable difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding when an unfulfilled oath to act is broken.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two oath types
Identify whether the oath is negative ('I will not do X') or positive ('I will do Y'). The ruling differs based on this.
Negative oaths require permanent abstention; positive oaths are fulfilled by one action.
2
Apply the linguistic rule: indefinite in negation vs. affirmation
In 'I will not do X', the act X is an indefinite noun in a negative sentence – Hanafi usul holds that nakirah in siyaq al-nafy indicates 'umum (generality). Therefore, any instance of X triggers the oath. In 'I will do Y', the act Y is indefinite in an affirmative context – this indicates a single unspecified instance.
This is the core proof (dalil) from the text.
3
Derive the consequences
For negative oath: doing it once breaks the oath and the oath is exhausted (no further breaking upon second act). For positive oath: doing it once fulfills; if never done, the oath is broken at the last moment of life when performance becomes impossible.
The text explicitly states these consequences.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that a positive oath is broken immediately upon failure to act, not at the last moment. Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
The text sides with Abu Hanifa.
5
Consider time-bound oaths and agency
If the oath is time-bound, breaking occurs at the end of the time if possibility remained. Fulfillment can also occur through delegation or even under forgetfulness/coercion.
These are additional details in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يفعل كذا تركه أبداً. ليفعلنه برّ بمرة. ولو حلّفه وال ليُعلمنه بكل داعر يدخل البلد تقيّد بقيام ولايته. بر بالهبة بلا قبول. لا يفعل كذا إلخ أي من حلف لا يفعل كذا، مثلاً: لا يأكل الحلو فعليه أن يتركه أبداً؛ لأن الفعل يقتضي مصدراً منكراً، والنكرة في النفي تعم فيتناول فرداً شائعاً في جنسه، فلو فعل مرة حنث، وينتهي اليمين حتى لو فعل ثانياً لم يحنث إلا في كلها (فتح عيني تركه أبداً : أي على الحالف أن يترك ذلك الفعل للدوام. (عيني) ليفعلنه بر بمرة [ أي برّ في يمينه بفعل المحلوف عليه مرة واحدة (عيني)] أي من حلف ليفعلن كذا ففعله مرة واحدة برّ في يمينه؛ لأن الملتزم فعل واحد غير معين؛ لأنه نكرة في موضع الإثبات فيختص، وإذا لم يفعله في عمره يحنث في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته؛ لفوات محل الفعل، خلافاً لأبي يوسف، ويبر بأي فعل ناسياً كان أو مكرهاً أو بطريق التوكيل فيما تجري فيه النيابة، وهذا إذا كانت اليمين مطلقة وإن كانت موقتة بوقت ولم يفعل فيه يحنث بمضي الوقت إن كان الإمكان باقياً في آخر الوقت، ولا يحنث إن لم يبق بأن وقع اليأس.
He does not do such-and-such: he leaves it forever. He must do it: he fulfills by once. And if a governor made him swear to inform him of every corrupt person entering the town, it is tied to the duration of his authority. He fulfills by gift without acceptance. He does not do such-and-such, etc.: That is, whoever swears not to do such-and-such, for example: 'I will not eat sweets,' he must leave it forever; because the action implies an indefinite infinitive, and the indefinite in negation is general, so it includes any individual of its kind. So if he does it once, he breaks the oath, and the oath ends, such that even if he does it a second time, he does not break the oath except in all of it. He leaves it forever: that is, the swearer must leave that action permanently. He must do it: he fulfills by once [i.e., he fulfills his oath by doing the sworn act once]. That is, whoever swears to do such-and-such, and does it once, he fulfills his oath; because the obligation is one unspecified action; because it is indefinite in an affirmative context, so it is specific. If he does not do it in his lifetime, he breaks the oath in the last part of his life due to the loss of the opportunity for the action, contrary to Abu Yusuf. He fulfills by any action, whether forgetfully, under coercion, or by delegation in matters where agency is permissible. This is if the oath is absolute. If it is time-bound and he does not do it within the time, he breaks the oath at the end of the time if possibility remains at the last moment; he does not break if hope is lost.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents two key rulings: (1) An oath not to do something requires permanent abandonment. The reasoning is that the forbidden action is expressed as an indefinite noun in a negative context, which implies generality (all instances). Therefore, doing it once breaks the oath, and after that, the oath is exhausted; doing it again does not break it. (2) An oath to do something is fulfilled by doing it once. The sworn act is indefinite in an affirmative context, so it refers to a single instance. If the swearer never performs it, he breaks the oath at the last moment of his life when it becomes impossible. This differs from Abu Yusuf, who held that the oath is broken immediately upon failure. The text also clarifies that the action can be done forgetfully, under duress, or via an agent if agency is valid. If the oath is time-bound, breaking occurs at the end of the time if the action was still possible at the last moment; otherwise, if hope is lost earlier (e.g., the person dies), it breaks then.

Key Terms
نكرة في النفي (nakirah fī al-nafy): An indefinite noun in a negative sentence, which implies generality (all individuals of the kind).
نكرة في الإثبات (nakirah fī al-ithbāt): An indefinite noun in an affirmative sentence, which implies a single unspecified instance.
حنث (hanith): To break an oath, incurring the obligation of kaffārah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not speak to Zayd.' After a week, he speaks to Zayd once. According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of his oath?
A) He has not broken the oath because he spoke only once.
B) He has broken the oath and the oath ends; he may speak to Zayd freely thereafter.
C) He has broken the oath but must continue to avoid speaking for the rest of his life.
D) He must offer kaffarah and then continue avoiding speech until he speaks again.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath not to do something requires permanent abstention (any instance breaks), while an oath to do something is fulfilled by a single performance, with breaking occurring at the last possible moment if unfulfilled.

Oath to Inform a Governor: Tenure-Tied or Absolute?
Ruling on oath tied to official tenure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath to inform a governor and explain why it is tied to the governor's tenure, applying the principle of ya's (despair) to determine when the oath is broken.

Core Idea
An oath tied to office lapses when the office ends; breaking occurs only at despair, not mere delay.

This lesson examines a specific oath scenario: a governor swears someone to inform him about every corrupt person entering the town. The critical question is whether this obligation continues after the governor dies or is dismissed. The Hanafi school, in its relied-upon narration, holds that the oath is tied to the governor's actual authority, because the purpose (repelling harm through policy) ceases with authority. We will explore the reasoning, the intra-madhhab dispute with Abu Yusuf, and the cross-madhhab disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. Additionally, we will see how the general Hanafi principle that an absolute oath is broken only upon despair (ya's) applies here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath (yamīn) sworn by a person (the swearer) to a governor (al-wālī) to inform him of every corrupt person (dā'ir) entering the town. The ruling in the Hanafi relied-upon position (zāhir al-riwāya) is that the oath is wājib (obligatory) to fulfill while the governor is in office, but it lapses upon his death or dismissal.
The obligation is not perpetual; it is conditional on the governor's tenure.
2
Analyze the ta'līl (effective cause)
The reasoning (ta'līl) is that the purpose (maqsūd) of the oath is to enable the governor to take administrative action (siyāsa) to repel the harm of the corrupt person. Once the governor loses authority, there is no benefit in informing. Therefore, the oath is tied to the existence of that purpose.
This is a form of attachment (ta'yīd) of the ruling to the purpose.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
The source mentions a narration from Abū Yūsuf and Aḥmad (and also al-Shāfi'ī) that the obligation to inform continues even after dismissal, because the information might be useful if the governor is reinstated. However, the majority view (zāhir al-riwāya) dismisses this as a mere illusion (mawhūm) and not a valid consideration.
Abū Yūsuf's narration itself is reported inconsistently: one version says the oath lapses only upon death, not dismissal.
4
Apply the ya's (despair) principle
The text clarifies that in an absolute (muṭlaqa) oath, breaking (ḥanīth) does not occur merely by failing to perform. Instead, the oath is broken only when the swearer reaches a state of despair (ya's) — when it becomes impossible to fulfill, such as upon the death of the governor or the swearer, or upon the governor's dismissal (according to the relied-upon view). Until that moment, the swearer is not considered a breaker even if he delays.
This is a key Hanafi rule in oaths: 'al-yamīn al-muṭlaqa lā tuḥnithu bi-mujarrad at-tark bal bi-al-ya's'.
5
Determine the muftā bihī (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is the zāhir al-riwāya view: the oath is tied to the governor's tenure; upon death or dismissal, the oath lapses and there is no further obligation to inform. The swearer does not break the oath unless an event causing ya's occurs.
This is the position given weight by the text's phrasing 'في ظاهر الرواية' and its refutation of the opposing view as based on mawhūm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلّفه وال ليُعلمنه بكل داعر يدخل البلد تقيّد بقيام ولايته. ... أي لو حلف رجلاً. (عيني) وال: أي متولي أمر بلده (عيني) بكل: داعر بالدال المهملة أي مفسد خبيث. (عيني) تقيد بقيام إلخ: أي لو حلف الوالي رجلاً ليعلمنه بكل مفسد دخل البلد فاليمين يتقيد بحال ولاية المحلف؛ لأن المقصود منه دفع شره بالسياسة، وبعد زوال الولاية لا فائدة في الإعلام وزوال الولاية يكون بالموت، وكذا بالعزل في ظاهر الرواية، فإذا زالت الولاية ارتفعت اليمين وفي رواية عن أبي يوسف وأحمد وقول الشافعي لا يختص الإعلام بقيام ولايته، فيجب على المستحلف الرفع إلى الوالي بعد العزل؛ لأنه مفيد في الجملة؛ لاحتمال أنه يولى ثانياً فيؤدب الداعر، وجه ظاهر الرواية: أن هذا الاحتمال فلا موهوم وعن أبي يوسف يبطل الرفع بموته لا بعزله ثم إن الحالف لو علم الداعر ولم يعلمه لم يحنث إلا إذا مات هو أو المحلف أو عزل؛ لأنه لا يحنث في اليمين المطلقة بمجرد الترك بل باليأس عن الفعل، والداعر بالمهملتين هو من قولهم: عود داعر أي كثير الدخان.
And if a governor made him swear to inform him of every corrupt person entering the town, it is tied to the duration of his authority. ... That is, if a governor made a man swear to inform him of every corrupt person who entered the town, the oath is tied to the state of the swearer's authority; because the purpose is to repel his harm through policy, and after the removal of authority there is no benefit in informing. Removal of authority occurs by death, and likewise by dismissal according to the apparent narration. So when authority is removed, the oath is lifted. In a narration from Abu Yusuf and Ahmad, and the saying of al-Shafi'i, informing is not confined to the duration of his authority; rather, it is obligatory for the swearer to report to the governor after dismissal; because it is beneficial in general, due to the possibility that he may be re-appointed and discipline the corrupt person. The basis of the apparent narration: this possibility is a mere illusion. From Abu Yusuf, the report becomes invalid by his death, not by his dismissal. Then, if the swearer knows the corrupt person and does not inform him, he does not break the oath except when he (the swearer) dies, or the governor dies or is dismissed; because in an absolute oath, he does not break by mere abandonment, but by despair of the action. And 'dā'ir' with two important letters is from their saying: 'ūd dā'ir' meaning much smoke.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage addresses an oath taken by a governor (or someone in authority) to inform about every corrupt person entering the town. The ruling is that such an oath is tied to the governor's tenure of office. Once the governor loses authority (by death or dismissal), the oath is no longer binding. The reason is that the purpose of the oath is to enable the governor to take administrative action (siyāsa) against the corrupt person, and that purpose ceases with the loss of authority. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf and Ahmad, and also the view of al-Shafi'i, that the obligation to inform continues even after dismissal, as the information might be useful if the governor is reinstated. However, the majority view (zāhir al-riwāya) considers this possibility too speculative. Abu Yusuf is reported to have said that the obligation ends at death but not at dismissal. The text also clarifies that if the swearer knows of a corrupt person but does not inform, he does not break the oath until an event (like his own death, the governor's death, or dismissal) puts him in a state of despair (ya's) regarding the possibility of informing. This is because in an absolute (unbounded) oath, mere failure to act does not break the oath; breaking occurs only when there is no remaining hope of performing the act.

Key Terms
داعر (dā'ir): A corrupt person; literally 'smoky' metaphorically referring to one who causes trouble.
يأس (ya's): Despair; loss of hope of performing the sworn act, which triggers the breaking of an absolute oath.
مطلق (muṭlaq): Absolute; an oath without a specific time limit or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A governor swears a person to inform him of every corrupt person entering the town. The governor is then dismissed. According to the Hanafi relied-upon position, what is the status of the oath?
A) The oath remains binding; the person must still inform.
B) The oath is lifted; no further obligation to inform.
C) The oath is broken immediately upon dismissal.
D) The oath is valid only if the governor dies.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school ties an oath to inform a governor to his actual tenure, and breaking occurs only when fulfillment becomes impossible (ya's).

Oath to Leave or Do an Act: Duration and Fulfillment
Ruling on oath to leave or do an act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the scope of oaths (negative vs. positive) and explain the grammatical reasoning involving the indefinite noun (nakirah).

Core Idea
An oath not to do X requires permanent abstention; an oath to do X is fulfilled by one act — indefinite in negation is general, in affirmation is specific.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on how oaths bind a person. The text clarifies that swearing to abstain from an act obligates permanent avoidance, while swearing to perform an act is discharged by doing it once. This distinction is based on a grammatical principle: an indefinite noun (nakirah) in a negative context indicates generality, but in a positive context it indicates a single unspecified instance. The passage also addresses a difference of opinion with Abu Yusuf regarding when a positive oath is broken if never performed, and covers practical scenarios like forgetfulness, coercion, and time-bound oaths.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the two types of oaths in the passage
Read the Arabic: 'لا يفعل كذا تركه أبداً' (negative oath) and 'ليفعلنه بر بمرة' (positive oath). Recognize that the ruling differs for each.
The negative oath is about leaving an act forever; the positive oath is about doing an act once.
2
2. Understand the grammatical principle (nakirah)
The passage explains: 'لأن الفعل يقتضي مصدراً منكراً، والنكرة في النفي تعم' (an action requires an indefinite verbal noun, and the indefinite in negation is general). Similarly, in the positive case: 'لأنه نكرة في موضع الإثبات فيختص' (indefinite in a positive context is specific). This is the proof (dalil) for the ruling.
This grammatical analysis is a distinctive feature of Hanafi usul for oaths.
3
3. Apply the principle to each oath
For 'لا يفعل كذا': the indefinite 'فعل' in negation means any instance of the action breaks the oath, but performing it once discharges the oath. For 'ليفعلن كذا': the indefinite 'فعل' in affirmation means one unspecified action fulfills the oath; multiple are not required.
If the oath is not to eat sweets, eating sweets once breaks the oath and the oath ends; if it is to eat sweets, eating once fulfills.
4
4. Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on timing of hanth
Abu Yusuf holds that if someone swears to do something and never does it, hanth (breaking the oath) occurs immediately upon abandonment (i.e., at the moment he decides not to do it). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that hanth occurs only at the last moment of life when it becomes impossible to perform the act.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
5. Consider extensions: forgetfulness, coercion, agency, time-bound oaths
The text states that an oath is fulfilled even if the act is done forgetfully, under coercion, or by an agent (if agency is allowed). For time-bound oaths, if the time expires and the act was possible at the end, hanth occurs; if impossible (e.g., despair), no hanth.
These rulings show the practical scope of oath obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يفعل كذا تركه أبداً. ليفعلنه برّ بمرة. ولو حلّفه وال ليُعلمنه بكل داعر يدخل البلد تقيّد بقيام ولايته. بر بالهبة بلا قبول أي حلفه أي قبول الموهوب له عندنا ليعلمن المحلوف الوالي الداعر لا يفعل كذا إلخ أي من حلف لا يفعل كذا، مثلاً: لا يأكل الحلو فعليه أن يتركه أبداً؛ لأن الفعل يقتضي مصدراً منكراً، والنكرة في النفي تعم فيتناول فرداً شائعاً في جنسه، فلو فعل مرة حنث، وينتهي اليمين حتى لو فعل ثانياً لم يحنث إلا في كلها (فتح عيني تركه أبداً : أي على الحالف أن يترك ذلك الفعل للدوام. (عيني) ليفعلنه بر بمرة [ أي برّ في يمينه بفعل المحلوف عليه مرة واحدة (عيني)] أي من حلف ليفعلن كذا ففعله مرة واحدة برّ في يمينه؛ لأن الملتزم فعل واحد غير معين؛ لأنه نكرة في موضع الإثبات فيختص، وإذا لم يفعله في عمره يحنث في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته؛ لفوات محل الفعل، خلافاً لأبي يوسف، ويبر بأي فعل ناسياً كان أو مكرهاً أو بطريق التوكيل فيما تجري فيه النيابة، وهذا إذا كانت اليمين مطلقة وإن كانت موقتة بوقت ولم يفعل فيه يحنث بمضي الوقت إن كان الإمكان باقياً في آخر الوقت، ولا يحنث إن لم يبق بأن وقع اليأس. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٤/١] (مستخلص)
He does not do such and such, he must leave it forever. He will do it, he fulfills it once. And if a ruler makes him swear that he will inform him of every corrupt person entering the city, it is restricted to the duration of his authority. He fulfills by gift without acceptance. That is, his oath. That is, acceptance of the donee according to us. The oath-taker is informed. The ruler. The corrupt. He does not do such and such, etc., meaning whoever swears not to do such and such, for example, not to eat sweets, then he must leave it forever; because the action requires an indefinite verbal noun, and the indefinite in negation is general, so it applies to a common individual of its kind. So if he does it once, he breaks the oath, and the oath ends, so much so that if he does it a second time he does not break it, except in all of them. (Fath: 'Ayni: He leaves it forever, meaning the oath-taker must leave that action permanently.' 'Ayni: He will do it, he fulfills it once [meaning he fulfills his oath by doing the sworn act once ('Ayni)] that is, whoever swears to do such and such, and does it once, he fulfills his oath; because the commitment is a single indeterminate action; because it is indefinite in a positive context, so it is specific. And if he does not do it in his lifetime, he breaks the oath in the last part of his life; due to the loss of the possibility of the action, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And he fulfills by any action, whether forgetful or coerced or by agency in matters where agency is allowed. And this is if the oath is absolute. And if it is time-bound and he does not do it within the time, he breaks it upon the passage of the time if the possibility remains at the end of the time, and he does not break it if it does not remain, i.e., despair occurs. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/374] (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage contains a fundamental ruling on the scope of oaths. The author first states that if someone swears not to do something, he must forever abstain from it; if he does it once, he breaks the oath, and the oath is discharged. Conversely, if he swears to do something, performing it once fulfills the oath. The reason is that the action is indefinite in the negative context (making it general) and indefinite in the positive context (making it specific). A dispute with Abu Yusuf is noted: if the oath-taker never performs the sworn positive act, Abu Yusuf holds that he breaks the oath immediately upon abandonment, while the established view (mufta bihi) is that he breaks it only in the last moment of his life when possibility is lost. The ruling also covers various scenarios: actions done forgetfully or under coercion, agency, time-bound oaths, and despair of performing the act. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it is from the core texts.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do or failing to do what one swore to do.
نكرة (nakirah): Indefinite noun; in negation it becomes general; in affirmation it remains indefinite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'By Allah, I will not eat sweets,' and then eats sweets once, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) He breaks the oath and must continue to avoid sweets forever.
B) He breaks the oath, but the oath ends, so he may eat sweets again.
C) He does not break the oath because he ate only once.
D) He must pay kaffarah but the oath continues.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a negative oath is broken by any single instance, after which the oath ends; a positive oath is fulfilled by one act, and if never performed, hanth is deferred until the last moment of life.

Oath by Ruler to Report Corrupt Persons
Ruling on oath restricted to duration of authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of an oath taken under a ruler's authority and explain the reasoning based on the purpose of the oath.

Core Idea
An oath to a ruler ends when the ruler's authority ends—the purpose creates the limit.

This lesson examines a specific case where a ruler makes an officer swear to report every corrupt person entering the city. The key question is: does this oath continue even after the ruler is removed from office? The Hanafi school answers with a pragmatic rule tied to the purpose of the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath to inform the ruler about corrupt persons entering the city. The ruling according to Zahir al-Riwayah is that the oath is restricted to the duration of the ruler's authority.
The obligation to inform is conditional on the ruler's tenure.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for this restriction is qiyas (analogy) based on the purpose (siyasah). No explicit text from Quran or Sunnah is cited.
The reasoning is aqli (rational).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the ruler's authority to discipline. When the authority ends, the purpose ceases, so the oath lapses.
This is a manifestation of the legal maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf has a narration that the duty ends only with death, not removal. Another narration agrees with the restriction. The mufta bihi is the restriction.
The tarjih is based on manifest purpose.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and other later scholars prefer the restriction because it aligns with the manifest purpose of the oath.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of istihsan (juristic preference) in oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلّفه وال ليُعلمنه بكل داعر يدخل البلد تقيّد بقيام ولايته. بر بالهبة بلا قبول أي حلفه أي قبول الموهوب له عندنا ليعلمن المحلوف الوالي الداعر لا يفعل كذا إلخ أي من حلف لا يفعل كذا، مثلاً: لا يأكل الحلو فعليه أن يتركه أبداً؛ لأن الفعل يقتضي مصدراً منكراً، والنكرة في النفي تعم فيتناول فرداً شائعاً في جنسه، فلو فعل مرة حنث، وينتهي اليمين حتى لو فعل ثانياً لم يحنث إلا في كلها (فتح عيني تركه أبداً : أي على الحالف أن يترك ذلك الفعل للدوام. (عيني) ليفعلنه بر بمرة [ أي برّ في يمينه بفعل المحلوف عليه مرة واحدة (عيني)] أي من حلف ليفعلن كذا ففعله مرة واحدة برّ في يمينه؛ لأن الملتزم فعل واحد غير معين؛ لأنه نكرة في موضع الإثبات فيختص، وإذا لم يفعله في عمره يحنث في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته؛ لفوات محل الفعل، خلافاً لأبي يوسف، ويبر بأي فعل ناسياً كان أو مكرهاً أو بطريق التوكيل فيما تجري فيه النيابة، وهذا إذا كانت اليمين مطلقة وإن كانت موقتة بوقت ولم يفعل فيه يحنث بمضي الوقت إن كان الإمكان باقياً في آخر الوقت، ولا يحنث إن لم يبق بأن وقع اليأس. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٤/١] (مستخلص)
And if a ruler makes him swear that he will inform him of every corrupt person entering the city, it is restricted to the duration of his authority. He fulfills by gift without acceptance. That is, his oath. That is, acceptance of the donee according to us. The oath-taker is informed. The ruler. The corrupt. He does not do such and such, etc.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This ruling addresses an oath taken by a ruler's order to inform him of every corrupt person entering the city. The Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that such an oath is restricted to the duration of the ruler's authority. Once the ruler dies or is removed from office, the oath is no longer binding. The reasoning is pragmatic: the purpose of the oath is to enable the ruler to exercise his authority to discipline malfeasants; once the authority ends, the purpose ceases. There is a reported view from Abu Yusuf and Imam Ahmad (and al-Shafi'i) that the duty continues even after removal, because information might still be useful if the ruler returns. However, the relied-upon view is the restriction, as it is based on the manifest purpose. The text also discusses variants in the manuscript regarding the phrase 'dakhala al-balad.'

Key Terms
وال (wali): A ruler, governor, or person in authority over a region.
داعر (da'ir): A corrupt, mischievous, or harmful person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a governor makes an officer swear to report every corrupt person entering the city, and then the governor is removed from office, what is the ruling on the officer's oath?
A) The oath remains binding until the officer dies.
B) The oath is no longer binding because the governor's authority ended.
C) The oath is binding until the corrupt person is reported.
D) The officer must still report to the successor.

Key Takeaway
An oath made to a ruler to report corrupt persons is automatically limited to the duration of that ruler's authority.

Oath on Gift vs. Sale: The Role of Acceptance
Ruling on oath fulfillment for gift vs sale with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on oaths regarding gifts and sales, distinguishing between fulfillment conditions based on the nature of each contract.

Core Idea
Gift oath fulfilled by declaration alone; sale oath requires acceptance — because gift is ownership without consideration.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a subtle but important distinction in oaths of performance. If someone swears to give a gift, the mere declaration of the gift fulfills the oath, even without the recipient's acceptance. In contrast, swearing to sell requires the buyer's acceptance for the oath to be fulfilled. Understanding this hinges on the nature of each contract: gift is a one-sided transfer, sale is bilateral.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its related contract
Determine whether the oath concerns a gift (hiba) or a sale (bay'). The ruling differs based on this classification.
Gift = transfer without compensation; sale = transfer with compensation.
2
State the default ruling for a gift oath
If the oath is to give a gift, it is fulfilled by the giver's declaration of 'I give it to you' (ijab), even if the donee does not accept.
The oath is about the act of giving, not the transfer of ownership.
3
Consider exceptions: oath not to give and presence/absence of donee
For an oath not to give, if the donee is absent, there is consensus that the oath is not broken by declaration alone. If present, the oath is broken by istihsan.
Zufar has alternative views (acceptance or possession required).
4
Contrast with sale oath
If the oath is to sell, the seller's offer alone does not fulfill the oath; acceptance by the buyer is required because sale is a bilateral contract.
Analogous contracts: lease, currency exchange, salam, pledge, marriage, khul'.
5
Note the ruling for corrupt (fasid) sale
If a corrupt sale is completed by both sides (offer and acceptance, plus delivery), the oath is broken, unlike gift where even a corrupt gift does not break the oath.
This is because fasid sale still involves exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بر بالهبة بلا قبول أي لو حلف أن يهب عبده لفلان فوهبه له و لم يقبل الموهوب له برّ في يمينه بمجرد قوله: "وهبت له"، ولو حلف أن لا يهب فوهبه له و لم يقبل، لم يحنث إجماعاً إن كان الموهوب له غائباً؛ لأن حضرة الموهوب له شره ط حنثه، وإن كان حاضراً حنث استحساناً، وقال زفر في قول: لا يحنث ما لم يقبل، وفي قول: ما لم يقبل و لم يقبض والعارية والصدقة والإقرار والوصية كالهبة وقوله بخلاف البيع" أي بخلاف ما لو حلف يبيع عبده فلان فقال له : "بعت عبدي منك" فلم يقبل لا يبر في يمينه؛ لأن الهبة تمليك بلا عوض، فيتم بالواهب والقبول شرط ثبوت الحكم، وهو الملك، وشرط الحنث الهبة لا حكمها، بخلاف البيع؛ لأنه تمليك من الإبهام، ونظير البيع الإجارة والصرف والسلم والرهن والنكاح والخلع، ويحنث بالفاسد من البيع أن يتم من الجانبين فلا والهبة. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٥/١] (فتح) بخلاف البيع: حيث لا يبرأ فيه بلا قبول. (عيني)
He fulfills by gift without acceptance. That is, if he swears to give his slave as a gift to someone, and he gives it to him and the donee does not accept, he fulfills his oath by just saying 'I have given it to him.' And if he swears not to give, and he gives it to him and he does not accept, he does not break the oath by consensus if the donee is absent, because the presence of the donee is [required for] his breaking? And if present, he breaks it by istihsan. And Zufar said in one view: he does not break unless he accepts, and in another: unless he accepts and takes possession. And loan, charity, acknowledgment, and will are like gift. And his saying 'contrary to sale' is contrary to the case where he swears to sell his slave to someone and says 'I have sold my slave to you' and he does not accept; he does not fulfill his oath; because gift is ownership without consideration, so it is completed by the giver, and acceptance is a condition for the establishment of the ruling, which is ownership, and the condition for breaking the oath is the gift, not its ruling; unlike sale, because it is ownership from ambiguity; and analogous to sale are lease, currency exchange, salam, pledge, marriage, and khul'. And he breaks the oath by a corrupt sale that is complete from both sides, but not gift. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/375] (Fath) Contrary to sale: where he is not absolved without acceptance. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between oaths concerning gifts and sales. If a person swears to give something as a gift (hiba), he fulfills the oath merely by declaring the gift, even if the recipient does not accept. This is because gift is a transfer of ownership without consideration, and the act of giving is completed by the giver alone; acceptance is only a condition for ownership to vest in the donee. The oath is about the act of giving, not the outcome. In contrast, if one swears to sell something, the sale requires acceptance to be valid, so the oath is not fulfilled until acceptance occurs. However, for a corrupt (fasid) sale where both parties have performed the exchange, the oath is broken. Also, for an oath not to give, if the donee is absent, there is consensus that he does not break the oath even if he declares the gift, because the gift is not deemed complete until acceptance or possession. If the donee is present, he breaks the oath by istihsan (juridical preference) because the declaration implies the gift. Zufar has alternative opinions. The same ruling applies to loan (ariyah), charity (sadaqah), acknowledgment (iqrar), and will (wasiyyah) as they are similar to gift.

Key Terms
بر (birr): Fulfillment of an oath (keeping one's word).
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath; incurring the obligation of kaffarah.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference; a method of legal reasoning that overrides qiyas for a more equitable result.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to give his book to anyone, and then declares 'I give this book to Zaid' while Zaid is absent, according to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he made the declaration.
B) No, because the donee is absent and did not accept.
C) It depends on Zaid's later acceptance.
D) He breaks the oath only if he also delivers the book.

Key Takeaway
A gift oath is fulfilled by the offer alone, while a sale oath requires acceptance; this stems from the unilateral vs bilateral nature of the contracts.

Oath About Smelling Basil and Flowers: Defining 'Rayhan' in Hanafi Law
Ruling on oath about basil not covering certain flowers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why smelling rose or jasmine does not break an oath not to smell basil, applying the Hanafi definition of basil and the role of custom ('urf).

Core Idea
Basil (rayhan) is a stemless plant; fragrant flowers on stems are not basil by Quranic and customary definition.

Oaths are a serious matter in Islamic law, and their interpretation hinges on the intended meaning of words. This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling: if a person swears not to smell basil, does smelling rose or jasmine break the oath? The answer hinges on the legal definition of 'basil' in the Quran and custom. We will explore the linguistic, scriptural, and customary reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core legal conclusion: a person who swears not to smell basil (rayhan) does not break the oath by smelling rose, jasmine, or violet. These are not considered basil in Hanafi law.
This is the explicit ruling from the text.
2
Analyze the Definition of Basil
Examine the Hanafi definition: basil is a plant without a stem (la saq lahu) that has a pleasant fragrance (ra'iha mustaladhdha). This definition is derived from the Quranic distinction between trees (shajar) and basil (rayhan) in Surah al-Rahman.
The Quran separates 'star and tree' from 'grain and basil', implying basil is not a tree.
3
Apply the Definition to Roses and Jasmine
Determine whether rose and jasmine fit the definition. They have stems, so they are trees (shajar) according to the Quranic usage. Their fragrance comes from their flowers, not the plant itself, so they are analogous to fruits like apples and quince. Thus, they are not basil.
Even if the flower has a pleasant scent, the plant having a stem excludes it from being rayhan.
4
Consider the Role of Custom ('Urf)
Custom is used to confirm or define terms in oaths. The passage notes that for violet (banafsaj), custom varies: in Baghdad, it refers to leaves; in Kufa, to oil. The ruling relies on local custom when the term is ambiguous.
If the swearer has a specific intention (niyya), that intention governs. Otherwise, custom determines the meaning.
5
Extend to Purchasing Oaths
The passage applies similar reasoning to oaths about buying violet or rose: buying the leaves breaks the oath because the literal meaning covers leaves, and custom confirms it. For violet, the custom of the locale determines whether leaves or oil is meant.
This shows the consistent application of 'urf in oath interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، مستخلص) والبنفسج والورد إلخ حتى لو حلف لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً فاشترى ورقهما يحنث، وهذا في العدد باتفاق الروايات؛ لأنه حقيقة في الورق وفي العرف أيضاً، فكان العرف مقرراً للوقوع على الحقيقة، وأما في البنفسج فيقع على الورق في عرف أهل بغداد، وأما في عرف أهل الكوفة فيقع على دهنه، فإنه إذا أطلق يراد به بائعه بائع البنفسج، وهو رواية الجامع الصغير أيضاً، قلت: هذا شيء يبتني على العرف، وقال الزيلعي: الياسمين قياس الورد لا يتناول الدهن؛ لأن دهنه يسمى ) زنبقاً لا ياسميناً. وهذا غير صحيح؛ لأن الزنبق اسم لزهر مشهور ، وفي أرض الشام منه كثير، وهو ورق أبيض وأصفر على غصن مستدق له رائحة ذكية ويعمل منه الدهن، ويسمى دهن الزنبق، وكذا الحناء تتناول الورق، هذا إذا لم يكن له نية، وفي "الكافي": الحناء في عرفنا تقع على المدقوق [رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٥/١] (فتح) ثم اعلم أن في شرح "مستخلص" وقع المتن هكذا: البنفسج على الدهن والورد على الورق ولم يساعد هذه النسخة نسخة أخرى، ولا تعرض لها أحد من الشراح غيره، فتركناها وألقينا النسخة التي هي مطابقة لأكثر نسخ المتن. (محشي) على الورق: أي يقعان في اليمين على الورق في عرفنا لا على الدهن. (ط)
He does not smell basil, he does not break the oath by smelling rose, jasmine, and violet, and rose upon leaf. That is, if a person swears... He does not break the oath by smelling rose etc. That is, whoever swears not to smell basil and then smells rose or jasmine does not break the oath; because basil is the name of a plant without a stem, having a pleasant fragrance by custom. Allah says: 'And the star and the trees prostrate' (al-Rahman 6), then says: 'And the grain with husk and the basil' (al-Rahman 12). So He made basil other than trees, so we know that whatever has a stem is a tree, and whatever has no stem is basil. Rose and jasmine have stems, and they do not have a pleasant fragrance in themselves; rather the pleasant fragrance is for their flowers, not for themselves, so they resemble apples and quince. And in al-Mabsut: he breaks the oath by smelling myrtle and what resembles it from basil. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) And violet and rose etc. So much so that if he swears not to buy violet or rose and buys their leaves, he breaks the oath. This is in quantity by agreement of narrations; because it is literally true for leaves and also in custom, so custom confirms the literal meaning. And regarding violet, in the custom of the people of Baghdad it applies to leaves, but in the custom of the people of Kufa it applies to its oil, because when it is mentioned absolutely, its seller is meant, i.e., the seller of violet oil, and this is also the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. I say: this is something based on custom. And al-Zayla'i said: jasmine by analogy with rose does not include oil, because its oil is called 'zanbaq' not 'jasmine'. And this is incorrect, because zanbaq is the name of a famous flower, and in the land of Syria it is plentiful, and it has white and yellow leaves on a slender stem with a strong fragrance, and oil is made from it, called zanbaq oil. Similarly, henna applies to leaves. This is if he has no intention. And in al-Kafi: henna in our custom applies to the powdered [henna]. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/375] (Fath) Then know that in the commentary of Mustakhlas, the text occurs thus: 'violet upon oil and rose upon leaves' and no other manuscript supports this version, and none of the commentators other than him addressed it, so we left it and adopted the version that matches most copies of the text. (Hashiyah) Upon leaves: i.e., both apply in the oath to leaves in our custom, not to oil. (T)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage deals with the scope of an oath concerning 'basil' (rayhan). The ruling is that if one swears not to smell basil, smelling rose, jasmine, or violet does not break the oath. The reasoning is linguistic and scriptural: basil is defined as a plant without a stem, with a pleasant fragrance. The Quran distinguishes basil from trees (which have stems). Rose and jasmine have stems, so they are trees, not basil. Their fragrance comes from flowers, not the plant itself, so they are analogous to fruits like apples and quince. However, some varieties like myrtle are considered basil. The passage also discusses oaths regarding buying violet or rose: if one swears not to buy them, buying their leaves breaks the oath, as the literal meaning applies and custom confirms it. For violet, custom varies: in Baghdad, it refers to the leaves; in Kufa, to the oil. The reliance is on custom. There is a textual variant rejected by the commentator. The overall principle is that oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf), and if no specific intention, the customary meaning governs.

Key Terms
ريحان (rayhan): Basil; a plant without a stem, with a pleasant fragrance, by custom.
عرف (urf): Custom; used to determine the meaning of words in oaths.
الآس (al-as): Myrtle; a plant considered a type of basil in Hanafi law as per al-Mabsut.
البنفسج (al-banafsaj): Violet; its meaning in oaths depends on local custom (leaves or oil).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the interpretation of an oath depends on the literal and customary meaning of its terms, as exemplified by the narrow definition of 'basil' that excludes stemmed fragrant flowers.

Oath Not to Smell Basil: Exceptions for Rose and Jasmine
Ruling on oath regarding smelling basil and purchasing its derivatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that smelling rose, jasmine, or violet does not break an oath not to smell basil, and explain the Quranic and customary proofs.

Core Idea
Basil is stemless; rose and jasmine are trees with stems, so smelling them does not violate an oath against basil.

When a person swears not to smell basil (ريحان), the key is to define what qualifies as basil. The Hanafi school relies on a Quranic distinction between basil (stemless plants) and trees (stemmed plants). This lesson examines that ruling, its proof, and how custom affects related oaths on purchase of flowers and oils. Understanding this helps clarify the interplay between sacred text and customary usage in oath rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the opening: 'لا يحنث بشم ورد وياسمين والبنفسج' — the oath is not broken by smelling rose, jasmine, or violet. Determine the act (smelling) and the ruling (no violation).
The ruling is negative: the oath remains intact.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The author cites verses 55:6 and 55:12, which distinguish between 'shajar' (trees) and 'rayhan' (basil). Extract the operative distinction: trees have stems; basil does not.
The Quranic context shows 'rayhan' is a separate category from trees.
3
Apply the distinction to rose and jasmine
Rose and jasmine have stems, so they are trees (شجر), not basil. Therefore, smelling them does not fall under the oath. The scent comes from their flowers, not from the plant body itself – analogous to apple or quince.
The ruling extends to any plant with a stem, regardless of fragrance.
4
Consider custom in oaths of purchase
The passage notes that for oaths like 'I will not buy violet', custom determines whether 'violet' means leaves (Baghdad) or oil (Kufa). Understand that custom overrides literal meaning when it is widespread.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that custom ('urf) can specify the referent of a term in oaths.
5
Refute conflicting opinions
Al-Zayla'i claimed jasmine oil is separate, but the author rejects this, noting that jasmine oil is called 'zanbaq' not 'jasmine'. Similarly, henna refers to leaves by custom. Recognize that Hanafi authorities resolve such differences by reference to prevailing usage.
The commentary reinforces that unbacked claims are discarded in favor of majority practice.

Unlike sale. One who swears not to smell basil does not break his oath by smelling rose, jasmine, or violet. And as for the leaves of the violet: if a person swears not to buy a violet, [he breaks it by buying the leaves]. A man who swears not to marry, and a fuduli marries him, and he ratifies by speech, he breaks his oath. The donee's condition for breaking the oath... And if the fuduli is present, he breaks the oath by istihsan. Zufar said in one opinion: he does not break it unless he accepts; in another: unless he accepts and takes possession. Loan, charity, confession, and will are like gift. The phrase "unlike sale" means unlike the case where one swears to sell his slave and says 'I have sold my slave to you' and the other does not accept – he does not fulfill his oath. Because gift is ownership without compensation, so it is completed by the donor, and acceptance is a condition for the establishment of the ruling (ownership), but the condition for breaking the oath is the gift itself, not its legal effect. Unlike sale, because it is ownership from vagueness, and analogous to sale are hire, currency exchange, salam, pledge, marriage, and khul'. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran and custom regarding oaths about smelling basil. The central ruling is that if someone swears not to smell basil (ريحان), he does not break his oath by smelling rose, jasmine, or violet. The reasoning is that basil, in the Quran and in custom, refers to plants without stems, while rose and jasmine have stems and are thus trees, not basil. The passage also discusses oaths about purchasing these flowers: if one swears not to buy a violet or rose, buying the leaves breaks the oath, because the leaves are the true referent of the name. However, differences in regional custom (Baghdad vs. Kufa) affect whether 'violet' refers to leaves or oil. The commentary includes a refutation of al-Zayla'i's view on jasmine oil and clarifies that henna likewise refers to its leaves. The passage concludes with a note on textual variation in the Mustakhlas commentary.

Key Terms
الريحان (ar-rayhan): In this passage, 'ريحان' refers to a plant without a stem, having a pleasant scent, such as basil (the herb).
الورق (al-waraq): Leaves of the plant, as opposed to oil or flowers.
الدهن (ad-duhn): Oil extracted from the plant (e.g., violet oil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to smell basil (ريحان), does smelling jasmine break his oath?
A) Yes, because jasmine is a fragrant plant.
B) No, because jasmine is a tree with a stem, not basil.
C) Yes, if the oath was made in Kufa.
D) No, but only if he intended jasmine specifically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath not to smell basil is defined by the Quranic distinction between stemless plants (basil) and stemmed plants (trees); thus rose, jasmine, and violet do not break the oath.

Oath Not to Enter a House: Rented and Borrowed Premises Included
Ruling on scope of oath regarding house: Hanafi vs Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school includes rented and borrowed houses in an oath not to enter someone's house, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'house' means dwelling, not ownership — custom overrides literal definition.

When someone swears not to enter a specific person's house, does the oath apply only to property they own, or also to any dwelling they occupy? The answer differs between madhhabs. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that custom (urf) governs the meaning of 'house', extending the oath to rented and borrowed residences, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view that limits it to owned property.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school says about breaking the oath when the person enters a dwelling that the individual occupies through rent or borrowing.
The oath is broken regardless of ownership.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the opposite position: the oath is only broken if the house is owned by the person.
Shafi'i relies on literal interpretation of 'house'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i dalil: the literal meaning of 'house' (دار) implies ownership, so the oath is tied to that literal object.
Literal meaning is the default in Shafi'i usul.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school rebuts this: the cause of the oath is enmity with the person, not the building; custom defines 'house' as dwelling, and the metaphorical sense is intended.
Hanafis prioritize custom (urf) over literal language when the context indicates a broader intent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: entering a rented or borrowed house breaks the oath in the Hanafi school but not in the Shafi'i school.
This affects whether the oath-breaker must perform kaffarah.

And his house by ownership and rent etc.: meaning if someone swears not to enter so-and-so's house, and enters a house inhabited by so-and-so, whether by ownership, rent, or borrow, he breaks the oath. Because what is meant by 'house' in custom is the dwelling. So he enters what he inhabits by any cause, by considering the general metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical meanings. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the scope of an oath not to enter someone's 'house'. The Hanafi ruling: if someone swears not to enter 'so-and-so's house', he breaks the oath by entering any dwelling that so-and-so occupies, whether he owns it, rents it, or borrows it. The reason is that in customary usage, 'house' refers to the dwelling place, and the oath's motivation is enmity towards the person, not the physical building. The Shafi'i school holds that the oath is only broken if the house is owned by the person, because the literal meaning (ownership) is intended. The Hanafi view is based on custom (urf) and the underlying cause (sabab) of the oath, which is personal enmity. The commentary also clarifies that 'by ownership' and 'by rent' are included.

Key Terms
الدار (ad-dar): House or dwelling; in oaths, it refers to the place where the person resides, regardless of ownership.
الإجارة (al-ijarah): Rental; a house taken on rent is included in the oath.
الإعارة (al-i'arah): Borrowing; a house borrowed (free loan) is also included.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, because the oath refers to the dwelling, not ownership.
C) No, unless Ali owns the apartment.
D) Yes, but only if the oath was made with the intention of the building.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'house' in oaths broadly as any dwelling the person occupies, based on custom and the underlying cause of enmity.

Debt Receivable Does Not Break an Oath of Having No Wealth
Ruling on oath of having no wealth when one has a debt owed by another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a debt receivable breaks an oath of having no wealth, and explain the basis for the dispute with the other schools.

Core Idea
A debt receivable is not wealth in hand for oaths—Hanafis look at present, not future.

An oath about one's wealth can be tricky when debts are involved. If someone swears he has no wealth while others owe him money, does he break his oath? The Hanafi school says no, because debt is not considered actual wealth in hand. This ruling differs from the other three schools, and the basis lies in how we define 'wealth' for oath purposes. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the reasoning, and the practical consequences of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school rules: the oath is not broken even if the debtor is solvent or insolvent.
Note the key phrase: 'lam yahnath' (he does not break his oath).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The other three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that the oath is broken because debt is considered wealth.
They treat debt as a form of property (mal) that can be collected.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand why the other schools consider debt as wealth: it has value and can be demanded.
Their reasoning is based on a broader definition of mal.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between wealth in hand (mal haadir) and a right in the dhimmah (liability). The oath considers present reality, not future possibility.
Doubt (shakk) about collection prevents breaking the oath.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the Hanafi school, no expiation is required; under the other schools, expiation for a false oath would be needed.
This affects whether the person must offer kaffarah or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حلف بأنه لا مال له وله دين على مفلس أو مليء لم يحنث. بالهمزة أي غني لأن الدين ليس بمال. أي رجل أي والحال أنه قد كان له [؟] فالحنث يلزمه بالنظر إلى المآل ولا يلزمه بالنظر إلى الحال فلا يحنث بالشك، وعند الثلاثة: يحنث. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٦/١] والمليء مهموز اللام بمعنى الغني والحاصل أن الدين على الغير سواء كان معسراً أو موسراً وصف في الذمة لا يتصور قبضه حقيقة فلا يحنث. (فتح) على مفلس بالتشديد وهو رجل حكم القاضي بإفلاسه. (عيني)
He swears that he has no wealth while he has a debt owed by an insolvent or solvent person: he does not break his oath. By the hamza, 'muli' means rich, because debt is not wealth. (Meaning: a man, and the situation is that he had...) So the breaking of the oath would be binding considering the future, but not considering the present, so he does not break by doubt. According to the three (other imams): he does break it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/376] And 'al-muli' is with a hamzated lam meaning rich. The conclusion: debt owed by another, whether the debtor is insolvent or solvent, is a description in the dhimmah (liability) that cannot actually be taken possession of, so he does not break the oath. (Fath) 'Insolvent' with shaddah: a man whom the judge has declared insolvent. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about an oath regarding wealth. The Hanafi ruling: if someone swears that he has no wealth (مال), while he has a debt owed to him by another person—whether the debtor is insolvent (مفلس) or solvent (مليء)—he does not break his oath. The reasoning is that debt is not considered wealth in hand (مال حاضر) for the purpose of such an oath; it is a claim on someone else's liability (ذمة) and cannot be physically taken possession of at the moment. The oath is about present wealth, not future prospects. Therefore, potential future collection does not make him break the oath. The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that the oath is broken because debt is a form of wealth. The Hanafi position is based on the distinction between actual wealth and a debt receivable; doubt (shakk) prevents breaking the oath. The commentary also defines 'muli' as rich (solvent) and 'muflis' as someone declared insolvent by a judge.

Key Terms
الدين (ad-dayn): Debt owed to the oath-taker by another person; not considered actual wealth (مال) for oath purposes.
مفلس (muflis): A person declared insolvent by a judge; one who cannot pay debts.
مليء (mali'): A solvent person, rich; one who can pay debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'I have no wealth' while he has a debt owed to him by a solvent person, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the debtor is solvent.
B) No, because debt is not considered wealth in hand.
C) Yes, if the debt is large.
D) No, only if the debtor is insolvent.

Key Takeaway
A debt receivable does not constitute wealth for oath purposes in the Hanafi school, regardless of the debtor's solvency, because the oath considers only what is currently in one's possession.

Oath on Entering a House: Custom vs Literal Meaning
Ruling on oath regarding entering a house owned, rented, or borrowed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on oaths regarding houses, state the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in a real scenario.

Core Idea
An oath not to enter a house is broken by entering the dwelling, regardless of ownership.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the definition of 'house' (dar) in the context of oaths. The Hanafi school, relying on custom (urf), rules that entering a rented or borrowed dwelling breaks the oath. The Shafi'i school insists on literal ownership. Understanding this difference clarifies how legal interpretation of common words affects rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: entering a house occupied by the person (whether owned, rented, or borrowed) breaks the oath.
The basis is custom (urf) – the word 'house' is commonly understood as the dwelling.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that only entering a house owned by the person breaks the oath.
The basis is the literal meaning (haqiqah) of 'house' as ownership.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the literal meaning is not exclusive; custom shows the word is used for dwelling, and the oath's intent (enmity) points to the person's residence.
Custom overrides literal meaning when it clarifies intent.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In Hanafi, entering a rented house breaks the oath; in Shafi'i, it does not.
This affects fatwa in daily life where houses are often rented.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi view is relied upon because custom is prevalent and the oath's context (anger/enmity) targets the person, not property.
Al-Ayni and later authorities prefer this view.

And according to al-Shafi'i: he does not break the oath except by ownership; because the literal meaning (ownership) is intended.

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on an oath prohibiting entry into another's house. The Hanafi position, as explained by al-Ayni, is that the oath is broken if the person enters the house that the other person occupies, regardless of whether the occupant owns it, rents it, or borrows it. The reason is that the custom (urf) understands the word 'house' to mean the dwelling place. Al-Shafi'i holds that only entering a house owned by the person breaks the oath, because the literal meaning of 'house' is ownership. The Hanafi school relies on the customary usage (urf) and the general intent of the oath (which is based on enmity towards the person, not the property).

Key Terms
الدار (al-dār): In the context of oaths, it means the dwelling place (masakin) by custom, not necessarily ownership.
الإجارة (al-ijārah): Rental arrangement for the house.
الحنث (al-hanith): The state of breaking an oath, requiring expiation (kaffarah).
