Oath and Debt: Does a Debt Count as Wealth?
Inter-madhhab dispute on oath and debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a debt owed to a person does not break an oath denying possession of wealth, and contrast this with the position of the other three imams.

Core Idea
A debt is not wealth for oath purposes; it is a right in the dhimmah, not tangible property.

When someone swears that he has no wealth while he is owed a debt, the jurists differ on whether the oath is broken. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the Hanafi position, distinguishing between present property and a mere claim, and notes the disagreement with the other schools. Understanding this distinction is crucial for oath rulings and expiation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the swearer does not break his oath (lam yahanth) regardless of whether the debtor is insolvent or wealthy.
2
Identify the opposing view
The three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that the oath is broken because a debt is considered wealth.
3
Analyze the opponent's proof
Their reasoning: a debt is an asset that will be collected, so it counts as wealth in a broader sense.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the oath relates to immediate possession; a debt is an obligation in the dhimmah, not tangible property in hand, so it does not break the oath.
This is based on linguistic and contextual interpretation of 'mal'.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi, no expiation is required; in the other schools, the oath is broken and expiation (kaffarah) is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حلف بأنه لا مال له وله دين على مفلس أو مليء لم يحنث. بالهمزة أي غني لأن الدين ليس بمال ... وعند الثلاثة: يحنث. رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٦/١] والمليء مهموز اللام بمعنى الغني والحاصل أن الدين على الغير سواء كان معسراً أو موسراً وصف في الذمة لا يتصور قبضه حقيقة فلا يحنث. (فتح)
He swore that he has no wealth while he has a debt owed by an insolvent or wealthy person does not break his oath. By alif (mali') meaning rich, because a debt is not wealth. ... And according to the three [imams], he breaks his oath. The summary: a debt owed by another, whether poor or rich, is a description in the dhimmah; its receipt is not possible in reality, so he does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath in which a person says 'I have no wealth' while having a debt owed to him by someone else, whether that debtor is insolvent (muflis) or wealthy (mali'). The Hanafi ruling is that he does not break his oath because a debt is not considered wealth in the present sense (bil-nazar ila al-hal); it is a right in the dhimmah (obligation) and not tangible property. The three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that he does break the oath because a debt is considered wealth prospectively (bil-nazar ila al-mal). The Hanafi reasoning is that since the debt cannot be immediately seized, it is not counted as existing wealth for the purpose of the oath. The text from Fath al-Qadir clarifies that whether the debtor is insolvent or rich, the debt is an abstract obligation in the dhimmah, so no actual possession is conceived.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): A debt owed by another; a liability in the dhimmah, not tangible property.
المُفْلِس (al-muflis): A person declared bankrupt by a judge, unable to pay debts.
المَلِيء (al-malī'): A wealthy person, capable of paying debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He breaks his oath because the debtor is wealthy.
B) He breaks his oath because the debt is an asset.
C) He does not break his oath because a debt is not considered present wealth.
D) He does not break his oath only if the debtor is insolvent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a debt is not considered wealth for the purpose of oaths, so swearing 'I have no wealth' while owed a debt does not break the oath.

Executing Hadd on a Sick Person: Stoning vs. Flogging
Conditions for executing rajm and jald on a sick person, with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sick person is stoned immediately if his hadd is rajm, but flogging is delayed until recovery, and explain the Hanafi response to the opposing school's proof from the hadith of the palm branch.

Core Idea
Rajm is executed immediately on a sick person; jald is delayed until recovery because hadd is a deterrent, not a destroyer.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on applying the hadd punishment to a sick person who has committed zina. The ruling hinges on the nature of the punishment: stoning (rajm) is considered a destruction-based punishment and is carried out immediately, while flogging (jald) is deterrent-based and must be postponed until the person recovers from illness. This distinction is supported by the general principle that hadd should not lead to unnecessary destruction. We will also explore a dispute with the Maliki, Hanbali, and some Shafi'i scholars who cite a specific hadith to argue that flogging can be adapted to the person's condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hadd
Recognize that hadd for zina is either rajm (stoning) for the muhsan or jald (lashing) for the non-muhsan. For a sick person, the ruling differs based on which hadd applies.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
According to Hanafi law, if the hadd is rajm, it is carried out immediately even if the person is sick, because the punishment intends destruction (itlaf). If the hadd is jald, it is delayed until the person recovers from illness, because jald is meant as a deterrent (zajr) and should not cause destruction.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Identify the opposing school's view
Malik, Ahmad, and some Shafi'is hold that the sick person should be flogged according to what he can bear, citing a hadith concerning a weak man who confessed to zina and the Prophet ordered a palm branch with 100 twigs to be used for one stroke.
This is based on the hadith: 'Take a palm branch with a hundred twigs and strike him one blow.'
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that the hadith applies only in cases where recovery is hoped for; if recovery is hopeless, flogging may be administered to the extent the person can bear. The general principle remains that jald is delayed until the person is healthy, to avoid causing death.
The Hanafi school uses the analogy of not cutting a thief's hand in extreme heat or cold, because it would lead to destruction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the Hanafi school, a sick person will not be flogged until he recovers, which could be a significant delay. Under the opposing view, flogging occurs immediately but in a mitigated form.
This difference ensures the Hanafi position prioritizes the preservation of life, while the opposing view prioritizes the immediate execution of the hadd.

والمريض يرجم، ولا يجلد حتى يبرأ والحامل لا تحد حتى تلد وتخرج من نفاسها لو أي الحامل كان حدّها الجلد. متعلق بقوله: وتخرج من نفاسها سياسة ظالمة فالشريعة تحرمها وسياسة عادلة تخرج الحق من الظالم وتدفع المظالم وتردع أهل الفساد ويتوصل بها إلى المقاصد الشرعية، فالشريعة توجب المصير إلى هذه السياسة أي العمل بها جائز شرعاً بالنسبة بالسلطان ونائبه إذا غلب على ظنه أن ظهور الحق يتوقف على العمل بها ولا يجوز أن يعمل بها القاضي والمفتي، وجه صحة السياسة في النفي أنه عليها نفى المخنث، ونفى عمر الله نصر بن الحجاج، وكان غلاماً صبيحاً تفتتن به النساء، والجمال لا يوجب النفي، ولكن فعل ذلك لمصلحة رآها، وقال في النهاية : المراد بالتغريب الحبس، وهو أحسن وأسكن للفتنة . من نفيه إلى إقليم آخر، ولهذا كان الحبس حداً في ابتداء الإسلام دون النفي، وحمل النفي المذكور في قطاع الطريق عليه رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۱/۱] (فتح) روي سواء كان والمريض يرجم إلخ: أي إذا زنى مريض، وحده الرجم يرجم؛ لأنه مستحق الإتلاف، فلا يؤخر بسبب المرض، وإن كان حده الجلد لا يجلد حتى يبرأ؛ لأن الحد شرع زاجراً لا متلفاً، ولهذا لا يقطع يد السارق في شدة الحر، ولا في شدة البرد؛ للإفضاء إلى الهلاك، وعند مالك في رواية وأحمد في رواية وبعض الشافعية: يضرب بحسب ما يحتمله؛ لما أن رجلاً ضعيفاً زنى، فذكر ذلك لرسول الله الله فقال علي: "اضربوه حده"، فقالوا: يا رسول الله ! ضعيف لو ضربناه مائة سوط ،قتلناه فقال علي: "خذوا عثكالاً فيه مائة شمراخ ثم اضربوه ضربة واحدة"، وعندنا: إذا وقع اليأس من برئه فيقام عليه بقدر ما يطيق. (عيني، فتح) حتى يبرأ: أي من مرضه؛ لأنه ليس بمستحق للهلاك وكان مانعاً. (عيني) والحامل لا تحد: مطلقاً حدها الجلد أو الرجم؛ لأنه يخاف الهلاك على الولد وله حرمة الآدمي وإن كان من الزنا؛ لعدم الجناية منه، ولما ورد من أنه عليه أخر الرحم عن الغامدية حتى وضعت حين أقرت بين يديه عليها بأنها حبلى من الزنا، وقوله: " وتخرج من نفاسها لو كان حدها الجلد"؛ لأن النفاس نوع مرض فينتظر البرء، ولما ورد عن علي له أنه أخر جلد الزانية حين أمره عليه بجلدها، فرءاها حديثة عهد بالنفاس وخاف الهلاك عليها فأخبره بذلك، فقال له عليها: "أحسنت"، وإن كان حدها الرجم ترجم بعد الولادة في الحال؛ لأن التأخير لأجل الولد وقد انفصل. وعن أبي حنيفة: تؤخر إلى أن يستغني الولد؛ لما روي . الغامدية أتت النبي عليه فأقرت بالزنا وإنها حبلى وأمرته أن يطهرها، فقال لها: "اذهبي حتى تلدي ثم أتته به بعد الولادة، فقال: "اذهبي فأرضعيه حتى تفطميه" ثم أتته به بعد أن فطم، (الحديث) ولأن في التأخير صيانة الولد عن الضياع، وهذا إذا لم يكن أحد يقوم بتربيته، ثم الحامل تحبس إلى أن تضع حملها إن كان ثبوت الحد بالبينة، وإن كان بالإقرار لا تحبس؛ لأن الرجوع عن الإقرار جائز. (مستخلص، فتح) 11 الجلد : لأنه يخاف الهلاك على الولد وإن كان حدها الرجم ترجم بعد الولادة في الحال. (عيني، مس)
And the sick person is stoned, and is not flogged until he recovers. And the pregnant woman is not punished (with hadd) until she gives birth and emerges from her postpartum bleeding, if her hadd is lashing. Connected to his statement: 'and emerges from her postpartum bleeding.' Oppressive policy, the Shariah prohibits it; and just policy extracts the truth from the oppressor, repels injustices, deters people of corruption, and achieves Shariah objectives. So the Shariah requires resorting to this policy, i.e., acting upon it is permissible legally for the sultan and his deputy when he is strongly of the opinion that the appearance of truth depends on acting upon it, and it is not permissible for the judge and mufti to act upon it. The validity of policy in exile is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) exiled the effeminate; and Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) exiled Nasr ibn al-Hajjaj, who was a handsome young man whom women were tempted by, and beauty does not necessitate exile, but he did that for a benefit he saw. And it is said in al-Nihayah: the meaning of 'banishment' is imprisonment, and it is better and more calming for fitna. And this is why imprisonment was a hadd in early Islam without exile, and the exile mentioned for highway robbers is interpreted upon this. (Raml al-Haqa'iq 1/381) (Fath) Narrated: whether it is... And the sick person is stoned, etc.: i.e., if a sick person commits zina, and his hadd is stoning, he is stoned because he is deserving of destruction, so it is not delayed due to illness. But if his hadd is lashing, he is not lashed until he recovers, because the hadd was legislated as a deterrent, not as a destructor. For this reason, the hand of the thief is not cut in extreme heat or extreme cold, because it leads to destruction. According to Malik in one narration, Ahmad in one narration, and some Shafi'is: he is struck according to what he can bear, because a weak man committed zina, and that was mentioned to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he said, 'Strike him his hadd.' They said, 'O Messenger of Allah! He is weak; if we strike him a hundred lashes, we will kill him.' He said, 'Take a palm branch with a hundred twigs and strike him one blow.' According to us, if there is despair of his recovery, then punishment is carried out on him to the extent he can bear. (Ayni, Fath) Until he recovers: i.e., from his illness, because he is not deserving of destruction, and it (illness) is a preventive. (Ayni) And the pregnant woman is not punished: absolutely, whether her hadd is lashing or stoning, because there is fear of destruction for the child, and the child has the sanctity of a human being even if from zina, because it has no guilt. And because it is reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) delayed [the punishment] of al-Ghamidiyyah until she gave birth when she confessed before him that she was pregnant from zina. And his statement: 'and emerges from her postpartum bleeding if her hadd is lashing' because postpartum bleeding is a type of illness, so recovery is awaited. And because it is reported from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he delayed the lashing of the adulteress when the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered him to lash her, and he saw that she was recently in postpartum and feared destruction for her, so he informed him of that, and the Prophet said to him, 'You have done well.' And if her hadd is stoning, she is stoned after birth immediately, because the delay was for the sake of the child, and it has separated. And from Abu Hanifah: she is delayed until the child is weaned, because it is narrated that al-Ghamidiyyah came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and confessed to zina and that she was pregnant, and she asked him to purify her, and he said to her, 'Go until you give birth.' Then she came to him after birth, and he said, 'Go and nurse him until you wean him.' Then she came to him after weaning. (Hadith) And because delaying protects the child from loss, and this is when there is no one to raise him. Then the pregnant woman is imprisoned until she delivers her child if the hadd is established by evidence, but if by confession, she is not imprisoned because retraction from confession is permissible. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 11 Lashing: because there is fear of destruction for the child, and if her hadd is stoning, she is stoned after birth immediately. (Ayni, Mus)
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed commentary on the conditions for executing hadd (prescribed punishment) on a sick person and a pregnant woman. The author begins by stating that a sick person who is guilty of zina is stoned to death immediately if the hadd is rajm (stoning), because stoning is a form of destruction and the person is already deserving of it; however, if the hadd is jald (lashing), the lashing is postponed until the person recovers from illness, because lashing is intended as a deterrent, not a destroyer. The rationale is that applying jald in severe heat or cold would lead to destruction, so it is avoided. This is the Hanafi position. The author then contrasts it with the view of Malik, Ahmad, and some Shafi'is, who hold that a weak person should be struck according to his capacity, citing the hadith where the Prophet ordered a weak man to be struck with a palm branch containing a hundred twigs in one blow. The Hanafi school responds that this hadith is specific to the weak man; the general principle is that jald is delayed until recovery, but if recovery is hopeless, then it is administered to the extent the person can bear. Next, the passage discusses the pregnant woman. The author states that a pregnant woman is not subjected to hadd until she gives birth and finishes postpartum bleeding if the hadd is lashing. If the hadd is stoning, she is stoned after giving birth immediately, because the delay was only for the safety of the child. The exception is Abu Hanifa's view, which delays stoning until the child is weaned, based on the story of al-Ghamidiyyah. The passage also notes that if the hadd is established by evidence, the woman is imprisoned until delivery; if by confession, she is not imprisoned because she could retract. This lesson demonstrates the principle that hadd execution is balanced between the necessity of punishment and the preservation of life and health.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): العقوبة المقدرة شرعاً على جريمة الزنا وغيرها من الجرائم الحدية
الرجم (al-rajm): القتل بالحجارة عقوبة الزاني المحصن
الجلد (al-jald): الضرب بالسياط عقوبة الزاني غير المحصن
المريض (al-marīḍ): من به مرض يخاف عليه من الجلد

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on flogging a sick person whose recovery is hopeless?
A) He is flogged after recovery, but since recovery is hopeless, he is never flogged.
B) He is flogged with a reduced number of lashes that he can bear.
C) He is flogged immediately with full force.
D) He is not flogged at all; the hadd is waived.

Key Takeaway
Rajm is executed immediately on a sick person; jald is delayed until recovery, or mitigated when recovery is hopeless.

Executing Hadd on a Pregnant Woman: Timing of Stoning and Flogging
Conditions for executing rajm and jald on a pregnant woman, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a pregnant woman's hadd is postponed until birth (and postpartum if flogging), and differentiate between the position of Abu Hanifa (delaying stoning until weaning) and the Sahibayn (stoning immediately after birth).

Core Idea
A pregnant woman's hadd is delayed for the safety of the child; for flogging until postpartum, for stoning immediately after birth according to the Sahibayn, or after weaning according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson addresses the timing of hadd punishments for a pregnant woman who has committed zina. The Hanafi school agrees that the punishment should not be carried out while she is pregnant, to protect the life of the unborn child. However, there is an intra-madhhab difference regarding stoning: Abu Hanifa delays it until the child is weaned, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that stoning should occur immediately after birth. We will also examine the evidence from the story of al-Ghamidiyyah and the practical implications of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for a pregnant woman
The Hanafi school rules that a pregnant woman is not subjected to any hadd punishment until she gives birth and, if the hadd is flogging, until she completes her postpartum bleeding (nifas). This is to protect the child, who has sanctity as a human being even if conceived in zina.
This applies regardless of whether the hadd is stoning or flogging.
2
Distinguish between flogging and stoning timelines
If the hadd is flogging (jald), the woman is flogged after birth and after the postpartum period ends. If the hadd is stoning (rajm), she is stoned immediately after birth, because the delay was only for the child's sake.
This is the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and is the mufta bihi.
3
Identify Abu Hanifa's differing view on stoning
Abu Hanifa holds that stoning is delayed further until the child is weaned (i.e., until the child is independent of breastfeeding). He bases this on the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah, where the Prophet told her to go and nurse the child until weaning.
The hadith is cited as evidence that the child's welfare necessitates the delay.
4
Examine the evidence for each view
The Sahibayn rely on the principle that the sole reason for delay is the child's presence in the womb; once born, that reason ends. Abu Hanifa extends the delay because the child still needs care, and he sees the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah as supporting this extension.
The text also notes the ruling on imprisonment: if hadd is proven by evidence (bayyinah), she is imprisoned until delivery; if by confession (iqrar), she is not imprisoned because she may retract.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Sahibayn's view, the woman is stoned a few days after birth. Under Abu Hanifa's view, she lives for up to two years (typical weaning period) before execution.
The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn (immediate stoning after birth).

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed commentary on the conditions for executing hadd (prescribed punishment) on a sick person and a pregnant woman. The author begins by stating that a sick person who is guilty of zina is stoned to death immediately if the hadd is rajm (stoning), because stoning is a form of destruction and the person is already deserving of it; however, if the hadd is jald (lashing), the lashing is postponed until the person recovers from illness, because lashing is intended as a deterrent, not a destroyer. The rationale is that applying jald in severe heat or cold would lead to destruction, so it is avoided. This is the Hanafi position. The author then contrasts it with the view of Malik, Ahmad, and some Shafi'is, who hold that a weak person should be struck according to his capacity, citing the hadith where the Prophet ordered a weak man to be struck with a palm branch containing a hundred twigs in one blow. The Hanafi school responds that this hadith is specific to the weak man; the general principle is that jald is delayed until recovery, but if recovery is hopeless, then it is administered to the extent the person can bear. Next, the passage discusses the pregnant woman. The author states that a pregnant woman is not subjected to hadd until she gives birth and finishes postpartum bleeding if the hadd is lashing. If the hadd is stoning, she is stoned after giving birth immediately, because the delay was only for the safety of the child. The exception is Abu Hanifa's view, which delays stoning until the child is weaned, based on the story of al-Ghamidiyyah. The passage also notes that if the hadd is established by evidence, the woman is imprisoned until delivery; if by confession, she is not imprisoned because she could retract. This lesson demonstrates the principle that hadd execution is balanced between the necessity of punishment and the preservation of life and health.

Key Terms
الحامل (al-ḥāmil): المرأة الحامل من الزنا
النفاس (al-nifās): الدم الخارج بعد الولادة
الغامدية (al-Ghāmidiyyah): المرأة التي أقرت بالزنا عند النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقصته مشهورة
الإقرار (al-iqrār): الاعتراف بالجريمة

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi of the Hanafi school, when is a pregnant woman stoned for zina?
A) Never; she is only flogged after birth.
B) Immediately after birth.
C) After weaning the child.
D) After the postpartum period ends.

Key Takeaway
A pregnant woman's hadd is postponed for the safety of the child; flogging until after postpartum, stoning immediately after birth (Sahibayn's view).

Theft from a Debtor: No Cut for Like of Debt
Ruling on theft from debtor and its legal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a debtor for the like of the debt and explain the role of istihsan, qiyas, and shubha in determining the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Stealing the like of a debt from a debtor is recovery, not theft—hadd is dropped by istihsan.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a nuanced scenario: what if a creditor steals from his debtor exactly what is owed? At first glance, this is theft, but the Hanafi school exempts it from the hadd of cutting. The reason lies in the unique nature of debt and the principle of istihsan. The ruling also covers deferred debts, excess amounts, and property of different types, revealing a sophisticated legal framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognize that the scenario is theft of an amount equal to the debt from the debtor. The ruling is: no cutting (hadd) is applied.
This is the core finding: the act is not treated as theft.
2
Distinguish between immediate and deferred debt
Note that the ruling applies equally whether the debt is due immediately or deferred. By qiyas, deferred theft would require cutting, but istihsan overrides this.
Istihsan here considers the entitlement exists; only demand is delayed.
3
Analyze scenarios of excess and different property types
If the stolen amount exceeds the debt, no cut applies due to shubha (doubt from co-ownership). If the property is of a different type than the debt, the ruling depends: cash (same genus legally) → no cut; merchandise → cut unless mutual consent.
The type of property matters; merchandise is not considered recovery.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that no cut applies in all cases, even for merchandise of a different type. The mufta bihi follows the majority view.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Synthesize the legal reasoning
The underlying principles: the creditor has a right to his property; taking a like amount is recovery; istihsan allows leniency; shubha blocks hadd. The hadd is a severe punishment and is suspended when any doubt exists.
This reflects the Hanafi tendency to restrict hudud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل دينه [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في السرقة من مديونه مثل دينه من جنسه والدين حال؛ لأنه استيفاء لدينه. (عيني)] أي ولا قطع على من سرق مثل دينه من مال مديونه؛ لأنه استوفى حقه، والحال والمؤجل في هذا سواء استحساناً؛ لأن التأجيل: التأخير في المطالبة، ويقطع قياساً فيما إذا كان مؤجلاً، وإذا سرق زيادة على حقه لا يقطع؛ لأنه بمقدار حقه يصير شريكاً فيه، فيصير شبهة، وإن سرق خلاف سرق من جنس حقه، فإن كان نقداً لا يقطع في الصحيح؛ لأن النقدين جنس واحد حكماً، وإن كان عروضاً يقطع؛ لأنه ليس باستيفاء، وإنما هو استبدال، إلا بالتراضي، وعن أبي يوسف لا يقطع. (مستخلص، عيني)
And like his debt [that is, likewise there is no cutting in theft from his debtor for the like of his debt of its type, and the debt is immediate; because it is recovery of his debt. (Ayni)] That is, there is no cutting on one who steals the like of his debt from the property of his debtor; because he has recovered his right. Immediate and deferred are equal in this by istihsan; because deferment is postponement of demand, and he is cut by qiyas if it is deferred. If he steals in excess of his right, he is not cut; because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner in it, so a doubt arises. If he steals something other than the type of his right, if it is cash, he is not cut in the correct view; because the two monetary types are one genus legally; and if it is merchandise, he is cut; because it is not recovery but substitution, except by mutual consent. And from Abu Yusuf: he is not cut. (Mukhtalaf, Ayni)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with legal reasoning from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq. It addresses a specific scenario: a person steals from his debtor an amount equal to the debt owed to him. The ruling is that the hadd of cutting is not applied. The reason is that the thief is considered to be recovering his own right, not committing theft. The same applies whether the debt is immediate or deferred by istihsan (juristic preference), even though qiyas (analogy) would require cutting for a deferred debt because the time for payment has not yet arrived. If the stolen amount exceeds the debt, the thief is not cut for the excess either, because he becomes a co-owner to the extent of his debt, creating a doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd. If the stolen property is of a different type than the debt (e.g., merchandise vs. cash), then if it is cash, there is no cut because cash types (gold and silver) are considered one genus legally; but if it is merchandise, cutting applies because it is not recovery but substitution. Abu Yusuf's view is that no cut applies in any case. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the first statement: no cut for the like of debt.

Key Terms
مثل دينه (mithlu daynihi): Stealing an amount equal to the debt owed to the thief from the debtor's property of the same type as the debt.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of leniency or practicality, based on a stronger evidence or custom.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents application of a hadd punishment, even if the act is technically unlawful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the Hanafi ruling if someone steals from his debtor an amount equal to the debt, but the debt is deferred (not yet due)?
A) He is cut, because the debt is not due, so it is not recovery.
B) He is not cut, by istihsan, because the entitlement exists, only demand is postponed.
C) He is cut only if the stolen property is of a different type.
D) He is cut only if the debt is immediate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a creditor who takes the like of his debt from the debtor is not subject to the hadd of cutting, due to istihsan and shubha, provided the property is of the same type.

Second Cutting for Repeated Theft: Unchanged vs Changed Item
Ruling on repeated theft of same object with intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the second cutting for stealing an unchanged item, explain the concept of shubhat al-suqut, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A second hand cannot be cut for stealing the same unchanged item because the first cutting leaves a doubt that repels the hadd.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced case of theft jurisprudence: when a thief steals the same item twice. The Hanafi school introduces a unique concept — the inviolability of the object (ismat al-mahal) — to determine whether a second cutting applies. We'll explore the ruling, the proof, and the disagreement with Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific scenario? A person steals an item, receives the hadd (hand cut), then later steals the same item again after it has been returned to the owner. The ruling: if the item is unchanged, no second cutting; if changed, second cutting applies.
The key condition is whether the item has changed (taghayyur al-ayn).
2
Locate the dalil
The opponents (Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i) cite the hadith 'If he returns, then cut him' as a general command for any repetition.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi response: the general command is qualified by the concept of shubha (doubt). The first cutting removed the object's inviolability (ismat al-mahal) for that thief; even after return, a doubt remains that repels the hadd.
Hudud are repelled by shubuhat.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold the opposite view: second cutting applies regardless of change.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute as well as inter-madhhab.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa: no cutting for unchanged items. This is based on the principle that doubts prevent hudud, and is supported by later Hanafi masters like al-Ayni.
The contrasting view is considered the qiyas but is not acted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشيء قطع فيه ولم يتغير : [ جملة حالية أي لم يتبدل ذلك الشيء المسروق عن حالته الأولى. (عيني)] أي لا قطع بسرقة شيء قد قطع فيه مرة و لم يتغير هذا الشيء كما إذا سرق عينا فقطع فيها وهي قائمة فردها إلى مالكها ثم سرقها وهي بحالها، لم يقطع، وعن أبي يوسف يقطع، وهو قول الشافعي، وهو القياس؛ لقوله عليه: "فإن عاد فاقطعوه" من غير فصل، ولأن الجناية الثانية متكاملة كالأولى، بل هي أقبح لتقدم الزاجر ولنا أن القطع أوجب سقوط عصمة المحل في حقه، فبالرد إلى المالك وإن عادت حقيقة العصمة لكن بقيت شبهة السقوط والحد يندرج بها، فإن تغير العين عن حالها مثل إن كان غزلاً فسرقه فقطع فيه، ثم رده إلى صاحبه فنسجه أو نحو ذلك ثم سرقه، يقطع فيه ثانياً؛ لأن العين قد تبدلت، ولهذا يملك الغاصب بمثل هذا التغير، فإذا تبدلت انتفت الشبهة الناشئة عن اتحاد المحل فيقطع ثانياً. (عيني، فتح)
And regarding something for which a cutting has [already] been executed and it has not changed: [a circumstantial clause meaning that stolen thing has not changed from its original state. (Ayni)] That is, there is no cutting for stealing something for which a cutting has once been executed and this thing has not changed. For example, if he stole an item, and his hand was cut for it, and it remained extant, and he returned it to its owner, then he steals it again while it is in that same state, he is not cut. And from Abu Yusuf, he is cut, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, and it is the qiyas, because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'If he returns, then cut him' without qualification, and because the second offense is complete like the first, indeed it is worse due to the prior deterrent. And our [Hanafi] argument: the cutting necessitated the removal of inviolability of the object in his regard; so upon return to the owner, even though the actual inviolability returns, there remains a doubt of its removal, and the hadd punishment is repelled by it. If the item changes from its state, such as if it was yarn, and he stole it and his hand was cut, then he returned it to its owner and he wove it or the like, then he steals it, he is cut for it a second time; because the item has changed, and for this the usurper (ghasib) acquires ownership through such a change. So if it changes, the doubt arising from the single object disappears, and he is cut a second time. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute. It deals with the situation where a person steals an item, has his hand cut (hadd), then later steals the same item again after it has been returned to the owner. The key condition is whether the item has changed or not. If the item remains exactly as it was, the Hanafi position is that the second cutting does not apply because the previous cutting has removed the inviolability ('isma) of that specific object in relation to that person. Even when the object returns to the owner, a trace of doubt remains, preventing the hadd. Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i argue that the second cutting is required because the hadith 'If he returns, then cut him' is general, and the second offense is a fresh crime. They see no reason to withhold punishment. However, if the item has changed (e.g., yarn woven into cloth), the object is legally different; the doubt disappears, and the second cutting is applied. This is also supported by the rule that a usurper (ghasib) can own an item if he changes it materially, indicating a new legal identity.

Key Terms
عصمة المحل (ismat al-mahal): The inviolability or protected status of the stolen object with respect to the thief; it is lost once the hadd is applied.
شبهة السقوط (shubhat al-suqut): A doubt about the continued protection of the object due to the previous cutting, which blocks the hadd.
تغير العين (taghayyur al-ayn): A material change in the stolen object's form or substance that alters its legal identity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented, when does the second cutting apply for stealing the same object?
A) Whenever the same object is stolen again, regardless of change.
B) Only if the object has undergone a material change, like being woven from yarn into cloth.
C) Never, because the first cutting removes all future liability for that object.
D) Only if the object is stolen from a mahram relative.

Key Takeaway
A second hadd cutting for the same object is applied only if the object has materially changed, because the doubt from the first cutting otherwise repels the punishment.

Items Subject to Cutting: Valuable Woods and Gems
Ruling enumeration with intra-Hanafi dispute on pearls and gems

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which valuable natural items are subject to hadd cutting in the Hanafi school and distinguish the unique condition for worked ivory.

Core Idea
Cutting applies to valuable, guarded items even if from a mubah origin, but raw ivory is exempt.

This lesson covers the Hanafi fiqh of theft regarding specific valuable natural items such as teak, sandalwood, gemstones, and pearls. The passage enumerates items subject to and exempt from the hadd punishment of amputation, and reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether items with an initially free origin (like pearls) incur cutting. We will examine the conditions of value and protection that govern the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items and their categories
List the items mentioned: teak, qana (spear wood), ebony, sandalwood, green gemstones, ruby, chrysolite, pearls, ivory, glass, oud, musk, oils, saffron, amber. Group them as woods, gems, animal products, and other valuables.
The term 'green gemstones' is incidental; it includes all valuable gems.
2
Determine the ruling for each item
For each item, note whether cutting applies (yes/no) according to the main Hanafi position. Woods and most gems: yes. Ivory: yes only if worked. Glass: no. Pearls: yes (mufta bihi).
The ruling is based on value and protection, not origin.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The ruling is grounded in the items being 'mal muhraz' (protected valuable property) and not freely available in that form in Dar al-Islam. They are akin to gold and silver.
The condition 'muhraz' is key for hadd.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on pearls and gems
Muhammad (via Hisham) holds no cutting because origin is mubah; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold cutting because items are valuable and guarded. Identify the proofs of each.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the madhhab.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the two: cutting applies for pearls and similar gems. This is the operative view for fatwa.
The text states 'wa lahuma' and likens them to gold and silver.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الساج [ الساج بالجيم شجر عظيم جداً، ولا ينبت إلا في الهند ويحمل منها إلى سائر البلاد. (عيني)] والقناة خشب الرماح، والآبنوس بفتح الباء هرباً من اجتماع الساكنين والصندل خشب أحمر وأصفر طيب الرائحة، ويقطع فيها؛ لأنها أموال محرزة عزيزة عند الناس ولا توجد مباحة في دار الإسلام، وقوله: "والفصوص الخضر" التقييد بالخضر اتفاقي والمراد منها مثل الزمرد والفيروزج وغيرهما، والياقوت أحمر وأصفر وأخضر، وأعزها الأحمر، والزبرجد – بفتح المعجمة والموحدة - حجر أصفر ينفع من الصرع، ويقطع في هذه الأشياء؛ لأنها من أعز الأموال وأنفسها، فصارت كالذهب والفضة، ولا يوجد مباح الأصل في دار الإسلام، وفي "الاختيار": لا قطع في العاج ما لم يعمل، فإذا عمل منه شيء قطع فيه ولا قطع في الزجاج مطلقاً، وقيل: في المصنوع يقطع، ويقطع في العود والمسك والادهان والورس والزعفران والعنبر ؛ لكونها من أعز الأموال. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) والقنا: مقصوراً جمع ،قناة، وهي خشبة الرمح، ألفها منقلبة عن الواو. (عيني) واللؤلؤ : قال الفقيه أبو الليث في شرح الجامع الصغير وذكر هشام عن محمد لا قطع في اللؤلؤ والياقوت ونحو الفصوص؛ لأن أصله مباح كالزرنيخ ونحوه ولهما: أن هذه الأشياء من أنفس الأموال ولا توجد مباحة الأصل بصورتها في دار الإسلام غير مرغوب فيها، وهي محرزة فصار كالذهب والفضة. (عيني، فتح)
Teak [teak is a very large tree, and it grows only in India and is carried from there to other countries. (Ayni)] And 'qana' is the wood of spears, and ebony - with fatha on the ba to avoid two sukoons - and sandalwood is red and yellow wood with good fragrance, and cutting is applied in these; because they are protected valuable properties among people and are not found free in the land of Islam. And his saying: 'and green gemstones' – the restriction to green is incidental, and what is meant is like emerald, turquoise, and others. And ruby (yaqut) is red, yellow, and green; the most valuable is red. And chrysolite (zabarjad) – with fatha on the first and third letters – is a yellow stone that benefits epilepsy. And cutting is applied in these things because they are among the most valuable and precious properties, so they become like gold and silver, and they are not originally free in the land of Islam. And in al-Ikhtiyar: no cutting in ivory unless it is worked; if something is made from it, then cutting is applied. And no cutting in glass at all; and it is said: in manufactured items, cutting is applied. And cutting is applied in oud, musk, oils, saffron, and amber; because they are among the most valuable properties. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) And 'al-qana' is the plural of 'qanah', which is the wood of the spear; its alif is transformed from waw. (Ayni) And pearls: al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: Hisham narrated from Muhammad that there is no cutting in pearls, rubies, and similar gemstones, because their origin is free like arsenic and the like. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): that these things are among the most valuable properties and are not found free-origin in their form in the land of Islam, they are not undesirable, and they are guarded, so they become like gold and silver. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating items for which the hadd of cutting is applied (or not) when stolen. The commentary expands on the matn's list: teak, bamboo (qana), ebony, sandalwood, green gemstones like emerald and turquoise, ruby, chrysolite, and pearls. The reasoning is that these items are valuable and protected, not freely available in Islamic lands, so they are akin to gold and silver. For ivory, the ruling depends on whether it has been worked: raw ivory does not incur cutting, but manufactured ivory items do. Glass is excluded regardless. Oud, musk, oils, saffron, and amber are included due to high value. Regarding pearls and categories of gems, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad reported through Hisham that there is no cutting because these items originate from the sea and are originally free (mubah), similar to arsenic. But the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) is that cutting applies because they are valuable and are not found freely in that form in Islamic lands. The relied-upon view (the position of the two) is that cutting applies for theft of such items.

Key Terms
مال محرز (mal muhraz): A property that is protected and valuable, not left freely available.
مباح الأصل (mubah al-asl): An item whose original source is free (not owned), such as sea gems, which may affect the application of hadd.
التقييد بالخضر اتفاقي (al-taqyid bi al-khudr ittifaqi): The restriction 'green' in the matn is incidental; the ruling applies to all valuable gemstones regardless of color.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) There is no cutting because pearls are mubah al-asl (originally free).
B) Cutting is applied because pearls are valuable and guarded, according to the relied-upon view.
C) Cutting is applied only if the pearl is green.
D) There is no cutting because pearls are similar to glass.

Key Takeaway
Cutting applies for theft of valuable, guarded items even if they are originally mubah, but raw ivory is exempt until worked.

Cutting for Wooden Utensils and Doors
Ruling on cutting for theft of wooden utensils and doors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which theft of wooden utensils and doors incurs hadd cutting, and distinguish items that are exempt.

Core Idea
Manufactured wooden items are subject to hadd; raw wood is not. Doors require lightness and hirz.

In this passage, we examine the Hanafi ruling on cutting for theft of wooden household items. While raw wood is not protected, manufactured utensils like bowls and doors become valuable property. However, doors have additional conditions: they must be in a secure place and light enough to carry. The passage also clarifies that common items like rush mats and pottery are exempt, unless they are high-quality mats of significant value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items
The passage discusses wooden utensils and doors. Raw wood is not subject to cutting. Recognize that manufacturing transforms the property.
2
Apply the principle of multahiqah bi al-sina'ah
Manufactured items are attached by craft and become valuable like other protected properties. This is the reason (illah) for applying hadd.
This principle is key in Hanafi theft law.
3
Check conditions for doors
For doors, cutting applies only if they are in a hirz (secure place) and light enough for one person to carry. Heavy doors are not desired and thus not subject to hadd.
Fixed doors (attached to building) are not protected.
4
Compare exemptions
The passage lists items not subject to cutting: rush mats (ordinary), bricks, pottery. However, high-quality Baghdadi and Alexandrian mats are subject if nisab is reached.
This shows that value and customary desirability affect the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأواني، والأبواب المتخذة من الخشب. فصل في الحرز الأواني والأبواب إلخ: قوله: "المتخذة" متعلق بـ"الأواني والأبواب" أي وكذا يقطع في الأواني والأبواب الخشب مع أنه لا يقطع في الخشب كما مر ؛ لأنها ملتحقة بالصنعة بالأموال النفيسة، حتى تكون من محرزة كسائر الأموال، ويقطع في الأبواب المعمولة إذا كان في حرز وكان خفيفاً لا يثقل على الواحد حمله؛ لأنه لا يرغب في سرقة الأبواب الثقيلة وإن كانت مركبة على الباب لا يقطع فيها؛ لأنها غير محرزة، وكذا لا يقطع في البواري والآجر والفخار بخلاف الحصير البغدادي والإسكندراني حيث قالوا: يجب القطع فيها إذا بلغت نصاباً. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٦/١] (فتح، مستخلص)
And utensils and doors made of wood. Section on hirz. Utensils and doors etc.: His saying: 'made' is related to 'utensils and doors', meaning likewise cutting is applied in wooden utensils and doors, even though cutting is not applied in raw wood as previously mentioned; because they are attached by manufacture to valuable properties, so that they become protected like other properties. And cutting is applied in manufactured doors if they are in a hirz and are light enough for one person to carry; because no one desires to steal heavy doors. And if they are fixed on the door, there is no cutting in that because they are not protected. Similarly, there is no cutting in rush mats (bawari), bricks (ajur), and pottery (fakhkhar), unlike Baghdadi and Alexandrian mats where they said: cutting is obligatory in them if they reach nisab. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/406] (Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on theft of wooden utensils and doors. It clarifies that cutting applies to manufactured wooden items like bowls and doors, even though raw wood does not incur hadd. The reason is that manufacturing transforms them into valuable protected property. However, for doors, additional conditions apply: the door must be in a secure place (hirz) and light enough to be carried by one person; heavy doors are not typically stolen, so cutting is not applied. If the door is attached to the wall, it is not considered protected. Examples of items not subject to cutting include rush mats (bawari), bricks, and pottery, except for high-quality mats (Baghdadi and Alexandrian) which are valuable and thus cutting applies if the nisab is reached.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is protected, such as a house or shop.
ملتحقة بالصنعة (multahiqah bi al-sina'ah): Attached by manufacture: raw material transformed into a valuable item through craftsmanship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is cutting applied for theft of a wooden door?
A) The door must be made of teak wood.
B) The door must be in a hirz and light enough for one person to carry.
C) The door must be attached to the building.
D) Cutting is never applied for doors.

Key Takeaway
Manufactured wooden items are protected, but doors must be light and in a secure place; heavy or fixed doors are excepted, as are ordinary mats and pottery.

Theft from a Permissioned Premises: Day vs. Night
Explains when amputation is not applied due to implied permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that permission to enter negates hirz during the day but not at night, and identify when amputation is wajib.

Core Idea
Permission to enter during day lifts hirz; at night, the building's purpose as a safe holds.

The Hanafi school has a nuanced position on theft from places like shops and inns. The key is not just whether the door is unlocked, but the nature of permission and the time of theft. This passage explains why daytime theft from such places does not trigger amputation, while nighttime theft does, and also addresses the situation of an unauthorized person entering a permitted group.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise and the ruling
Locate the 'house with permission to enter' and note the two-part ruling: no amputation during day, amputation at night.
2
Understand the 'illah (ratio legis)
Trace why permission matters: it makes the place not a hirz. Then see why night changes: the building is meant to safeguard wealth, and permission is only implied for daytime.
3
Apply to scenarios
Test the rule: a shop open during day → no amputation; a shop at night → amputation; an unpermitted person entering with a permitted group → amputation.
The unpermitted person has no shield of permission.
4
Connect to broader principle
Note that this ruling is part of a series: the phrase 'he is not amputated' applies to all prior issues about theft from relatives etc., showing a consistent principle.
The passage's final note links back to earlier text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيت أذن في دخوله لم يقطع. أي لا يقطع الشيء المسروق. وبيت أذن في دخوله أي إذا سرق من بيت قد أذن للناس بالدخول فيه كحوانيت التجار والخانات لا يقطع؛ لوجود الإذن حقيقة، وهذا إذا كانت السرقة نهاراً، فلو سرق منها ليلاً يقطع؛ لأنها بنيت لإحراز الأموال، وإنما خص الإذن بالنهار، ولو أذن الجماعة مخصوصين فدخل معهم من لم يؤذن له وسرق ينبغي أن يقطع؛ وقوله: " لم يقطع" جواب المسائل المذكورة كلها كما بيناه تحت كل مسألة (مستخلص، عيني) لم يقطع: خبر أو جزاء للمسائل المذكورة كلها من قوله: ذي رحم محرم" إلى "بيت أذن في دخوله" كما بيناه. (محشي)
And a house in which entry is permitted: he is not amputated. That is, the stolen item is not amputated. And 'a house in which entry is permitted' means if he steals from a house where people are permitted to enter, like merchants' shops and inns, he is not amputated, because permission exists actually. This is if the theft is during the day. If he steals from it at night, he is amputated, because they are built for safeguarding wealth, and permission is only specific to daytime. If a specific group is permitted, and one who was not permitted enters with them and steals, it is appropriate that he should be amputated. And his words 'he is not amputated' is the response to all the mentioned issues, as we explained under each issue. (Mukhtalas, Ayni) 'He is not amputated': predicate or consequence for all the mentioned issues from his words 'dhii rahim muhram' to 'a house in which entry is permitted', as we explained. (Mahshi)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation regarding theft from a place where entry is permissioned. The ruling states that amputation is not applied if the thief steals from a house where people are generally allowed to enter, such as shops and inns, because the permission implies that the owner does not treat the place as a complete hirz (secure enclosure). However, this only applies if the theft occurs during the day. If it happens at night, amputation is necessary because such places are built for safeguarding wealth, and the permission is only understood for daytime. The passage also addresses a scenario where a specific group is permitted entry, but an unpermitted person joins them and steals; in that case, amputation should be applied because the thief had no permission. The final note clarifies that the phrase 'he is not amputated' serves as the response for all previously mentioned issues (from earlier parts of the text), confirming that it is the general ruling for such cases.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure enclosure or place where property is kept and considered safe from theft. The text distinguishes between hirz by place and hirz by guardian.
إذن (idhn): Permission to enter, which negates the status of the place as a hirz for the thief during the day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Amputation is applied because the shop is a hirz.
B) Amputation is not applied because permission to enter during the day negates hirz.
C) Amputation is applied only if the stolen item exceeds the nisab.
D) Amputation is not applied because the thief had implied permission to steal.

Key Takeaway
Daytime theft from a place with general permission does not incur amputation; nighttime theft does, because the building's purpose as a safe overrides implied permission.

Two Exceptions to Amputation: Guest Theft and Theft Without Removal
Ruling statement on two exceptions to hadd amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions that exempt a thief from amputation and explain the rationale based on hirz.

Core Idea
A guest's permission negates hirz; theft is incomplete without removal from the entire property.

The hadd punishment for theft requires the stolen item to be taken from a secure place (hirz) and removed completely. This lesson presents two exceptions: first, when the thief is a guest, the hirz is nullified due to his lawful entry; second, when the item is not taken out of the house, the act is incomplete. Understanding these exceptions clarifies the fiqh of hirz and the completion of theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage addresses (a) a guest stealing and (b) theft without removal from the house.
2
Analyze the guest exception
Note that the guest's permission to enter removes the hirz even from locked containers. The house and its contents are no longer secure in relation to the guest.
3
Analyze the incomplete removal exception
Understand that a small house is one hirz; removal from the entire house is required for amputation. For a large house with separate courtyards, removal from the courtyard suffices.
4
Apply the distinction between small and large house
Recognize that the ruling differs based on the layout: a small house requires removal from the entire structure; a large house only requires removal from the specific section (courtyard).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضيف إلخ : أي إذا سرق ضيف من مال من أضاف ذلك الشخص لا يقطع، سواء سرق من البيت الذي أضافه تلك الدار التي أذن له في دخولها وهو مقفل، أو من صندوق مقفل؛ لأن البيت لم يبق حرزاً في حقه؛ لكونه مأذونا في دخوله وكونه بمنزلة أهل الدار، وقوله : "أو سرق شيئاً ولم يخرجه" أي إذا سرق سرقة ولم يخرج المسروق من الدار لا يقطع؛ لأن الدار كلها حرز واحد فلم يتحقق الأخذ من كل وجه، فلا بد من الإخراج منها، وهذا إذا كانت الدار صغيرة لا يستغني أهل البيت عن الانتفاع بصحنها، وإن كانت كبيرة وأخرجه أهل صحنها يقطع، وإن لم يخرجه منها.(مستخلص، عيني)
Guest etc.: that is, if a guest steals from the property of the one who hosted him, he is not amputated, whether he steals from the house where he was hosted, the house he was permitted to enter, even if locked, or from a locked chest, because the house is no longer a hirz for him, due to his permission to enter and his being like the residents. And his words 'or he steals something and does not remove it' means if he commits a theft and does not remove the stolen item from the house, he is not amputated, because the entire house is one single hirz, so taking is not complete in every respect; therefore removal from it is necessary. This is if the house is small such that the residents cannot do without using its courtyard. If it is large and he removes it from the courtyard, he is amputated even if he does remove it from the whole house. (Mukhtalas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings. First, regarding a guest: a guest who steals from his host is not subject to amputation because the house, though a hirz for others, is not a hirz for him since he is permitted to enter and is treated like a resident. This applies even if the house or container is locked. Second, regarding theft without removal from the house: if a thief steals something but does not take it out of the house, he is not amputated because the entire house is considered one protective unit. For a small house where the courtyard is integral, removal from the house is required. For a large house with separate units, if the thief removes the item from a room into the courtyard, that is sufficient for amputation. The difference hinges on whether the house is considered a single hirz or composed of multiple hirz.

Key Terms
ضيف (dayf): A guest who has been hosted by the owner of the property. The guest is not considered a thief meriting amputation due to implied trust.
صندوق مقفل (sunduq muqfal): A locked chest. Even if it is locked, since the guest is permitted in the house, the chest is not a hirz for him.
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is protected; essential for hadd amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guest steals a valuable item from a locked safe in the host's house, what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) The guest's hand is amputated because the safe is locked.
B) The guest is not amputated because he is permitted in the house.
C) The guest is amputated because he is not allowed to open the safe.
D) The guest is not amputated because the item was not removed from the house.

Key Takeaway
A guest is never amputated for theft from the host's home, and theft without removal from the entire hirz does not incur the hadd.

Four Issues on Removal from a Large House
Details four theft scenarios in large houses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios in which removal from a compartment in a large house triggers the hadd penalty for theft, and apply the Hanafi definition of hirz in multi-room dwellings.

Core Idea
In a large house, each room is a separate hirz; removal to a common area is like removal to the street.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq details four specific issues that clarify when the act of removing stolen property from a large house is considered complete for the purpose of the hadd punishment. The key principle is that in a large house, each room is an independent secure place (hirz), so moving the stolen item from a room to the courtyard or the street constitutes full removal. However, the size of the house matters, and the passage also examines a trick where a thief throws goods out and later retrieves them, as well as the use of an animal to carry the goods out.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Recognize that in a large house, each room (maqsurah) is considered a separate hirz because residents of one room do not need to use the courtyard of another. This is evidenced by each room having its own lock.
This principle only applies to large houses; a small house has a single hirz for the entire structure.
2
Apply to the First Two Issues
First issue: stealing from a room and moving the item to the courtyard counts as full removal, leading to amputation. Second issue: raiding (igharah) from one resident's compartment to another also counts, because speed negates any claim of temporary possession.
Raiding implies swift taking, which confirms the thief was never in lawful possession.
3
Analyze the Third Issue and the Dispute with Zufar
If a burglar breaks in, takes goods, throws them into the street, and later retrieves them, the Hanafi view is that amputation applies because removal from the hirz is complete at the moment of throwing. Zufar argues that if he leaves the goods, they are abandoned, and his later retrieval does not create a new theft. The majority view considers the throwing a trick invented by thieves.
Contrast: If the thief abandons the goods and someone else takes them, the latter is not cut, but that does not affect the original thief's liability.
4
Analyze the Fourth Issue and Note the Small House Exception
If the thief loads goods onto a donkey and drives it out of the house, the donkey's movement is attributed to the thief, so it counts as his own removal. However, all these rulings apply to large houses; in a small house, any removal short of exiting the entire house does not trigger amputation.
The small house is treated as one hirz, so internal movement is not removal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أخرجه إلخ: هذا الشرط يشتمل أربع مسائل، وجزاء الكل قوله: قطع المسألة الأولى: دار كبيرة فيها مقاصير وحجر ومنازل فسرق رجل من حجرة وأخرج المسروق إلى صحن الدار يقطع؛ لأنه يكون إخراجه إلى الصحن كإخراجه إلى السكة؛ إذ كل حجرة باعتبار ساكنها حرز على حدة، ألا ترى أن على باب كل مقصورة - غلقاً على حدة، والمقصورة مكان يستغني به أهله عن الانتفاع بصحن الدار، والثانية: أغار من أهل المقاصير من على مقصورة وسرق منها شيئاً قطع؛ لأن كل حجرة حرز على حدة، والمراد بالإغارة الإسراع من الأخذ "أغار الثعلب والفرس إغارة" إذا أسرع في العدو، وهذا أيضاً فيما إذا كانت الدار كبيرة؛ لأنها بمنزلة المحلة، بخلاف ما إذا كانت صغيرة، فإن حكمها حكم مكان واحد، فلا يقطع الساكن ولا المأذون له بالدخول فيها إذا سرق من بعض مقاصيرها، والثالثة: إذا نقب اللص البيت فأخذ شيئاً من متاعه، فألقاه في الطريق ثم خرج ثم أخذه، يقطع عندنا، وقال زفر : لا يقطع؛ لأنه إن رماه وتركه وأخذه غيره لا يقطع الآخذ. فكذا إذا أخذه بنفسه، ولنا أنه يخرج من الحرز بنفسه فتمت السرقة والرمي حيلة يعتاده السراق؛ لتعذر الخروج مع المتاع أو ليتفرغ لدفع صاحب الدار أو للفرار، بخلاف ما إذا تركه؛ لأنه مضيع لا سارق، والرابعة: أن السارق دخل الدار وأخذ المتاع فحمله على حمار فساق وأخرجه من الدار قطع؛ لأن سير الحمار مضاف إليه؛ لسوقه فصار كأنه أخرجه بنفسه [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٧/١] (مستخلص)
And if he removes it etc.: This condition includes four issues, and the consequence of all is his statement: he is cut off. First issue: a large house containing compartments, rooms, and dwellings; if a man steals from a room and removes the stolen item to the courtyard of the house, he is cut off, because his removal to the courtyard is like removal to the street, since each room, by virtue of its resident, is a separate hirz. Do you not see that each compartment has its own lock? And the compartment is a place whose residents do not need to use the courtyard. Second issue: if one from the residents of the compartments raids another compartment and steals something from it, he is cut off, because each room is a separate hirz. The meaning of 'raiding' is to take quickly—'the fox and horse raid' means they speed in running. This also applies when the house is large, because it is like a neighborhood; unlike a small house, whose ruling is that of a single place, so the resident or the one permitted to enter is not cut off if he steals from some of its compartments. Third issue: if the burglar breaks into the house and takes some of its goods, then throws it into the street, then goes out and takes it, he is cut off in our view, but Zufar says he is not cut off, because if he throws it and leaves it and someone else takes it, the latter is not cut off. So likewise if he takes it himself. But our proof: he removes it from the hirz by himself, so the theft is complete, and the throwing is a trick commonly used by thieves due to the difficulty of exiting with the goods or to free himself to resist the owner or to flee; unlike if he leaves it, because he is a waster, not a thief. Fourth issue: the thief enters the house, takes the goods, loads them onto a donkey, drives it, and removes it from the house—he is cut off, because the donkey's movement is attributed to him due to his driving, so it is as if he removed it himself. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/407] (Mukhtalas)
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage outlines four specific cases where removal of stolen property from a large house results in amputation. First, if a thief steals from a room in a large house and moves the item to the courtyard, it is considered full removal because each room is its own secure unit. Second, if a resident of one compartment raids another compartment within the same large house, amputation applies because each room is a separate hirz. Third, if a burglar breaks in, takes goods, throws them out to the street, and later retrieves them, our Hanafi view is that amputation is required because the removal from the hirz is complete at the moment of throwing; the subsequent retrieval is just reclaiming. This counters the opinion of Zufar, who argued the thief could abandon the item. Fourth, if the thief loads goods onto a donkey and drives it out of the house, the donkey's motion is attributed to the thief, so it counts as his own removal. The overall principle is that in a large house (like a multi-story building), each room is a separate hirz, so any movement between rooms is a violation of a hirz, but the complete removal from the entire house is not needed if removal from the room to a common area suffices.

Key Terms
إغارة (igharah): A quick, sudden taking of property, like a raid; used metaphorically for swift theft.
مقصورة (maqsurah): An enclosed compartment or separate room within a large house, considered an independent hirz.
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept; its definition varies by the type of property and location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a large house with separate rooms, if a thief steals a necklace from one room and drops it in the corridor, is he amputated according to this passage?
A) No, because the corridor is part of the same house.
B) Yes, because each room is a separate hirz.
C) No, because the thief did not intend to leave the house.
D) Yes, because the thief took it from the room.

Key Takeaway
In a large house, each room is a separate hirz, and removal to a common area is sufficient for amputation.

The Ruling on Handing Stolen Goods to an Accomplice
Covers scenarios where the thief hands goods to an accomplice or retrieves through an opening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario of munawalah and explain why neither the giver nor the receiver is subject to amputation.

Core Idea
Neither accomplice is amputated because the complete theft is not performed by one person.

In Hanafi criminal law, the hadd penalty for theft (amputation of the hand) requires that the thief both breach the hirz (secure place) and physically remove the stolen property. When a thief hands the goods to an accomplice, the act is split: one breaches, the other removes. This raises the question: who is liable? The passage presents four scenarios and the ruling that in principle, neither is cut off—unless one party completes both elements. Understanding this nuance prevents wrongful application of a severe penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Scenarios
The passage outlines four issues arising when a thief hands stolen property to another. The first two are explicitly described: (1) Burglar enters, takes item, hands to accomplice outside; (2) Thief inserts hand only through a breach and takes item. The text cuts off before completing the remaining two.
We focus on the first two complete scenarios.
2
Determine the General Ruling
The author states 'la'' (no cut) for all forms. The rationale: the inside thief did not complete removal because the property was intercepted before full exit; the outside man did not breach the hirz. Thus, no single person satisfies both conditions for amputation.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Dissent
Abu Yusuf holds that the inside thief is always cut off because he initiated removal. The outside thief's liability depends on action: if he inserted his hand and took, he is cut; if he merely received, he is not. This shifts responsibility to the active party.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the Ruling to the Second Scenario
When a thief only inserts his hand through a hole and extracts an item, the text cites a narration from Ali (r) that such a 'clever' thief is not cut off. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that removal was completed by the hand, thus amputation applies.
The passage ends before the resolution of this ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ناول آخر ويجوز أن يكون "آخر" بالرفع والمعنى: أخذ رجل آخر من السارق الداخل من خارج البيت، ويجوز أن يكون بالنصب والمعنى حينئذ: أعطى السارق المسروق رجلاً آخر، وهذا شرط يشمل على أربع مسائل أيضاً وجزاء الكل قوله: "لا"، أي لا يقطع في جميع هذه الصور، الأولى: نقب اللص البيت فدخل وأخذ المتاع وناوله الآخر خارج البيت، فلا قطع عليهما، أما المناول؛ فلأنه لم يوجد منه الإخراج؛ لاعتراض يد معتبرة على المال قبل خروجه، وأما الرجل الخارج؛ فلأنه لم يوجد منه هتك الحرز. مسألة فلم يتم السرقة من كل واحد وعن أبي يوسف أن على الداخل القطع على كل حال، وأما الخارج فإن أدخل يده وأخذ المتاع من الداخل يقطع، وإن أخرج الداخل يده وأعطى الخارج لا قطع على الخارج، وهذا بناء على نقب البيت وأدخل يده فيه، وهو المسألة الثانية وبه قال الشافعي الثانية: أدخل يده في بيت من النقب وأخذ شيئا لا يقطع، لما روي عن علي الله اللص إذا كان ظريفاً لا يقطع، قيل: كيف ذلك؟ قال: أن ينقب البيت ويدخل يده ويخرج المتاع من غير أن يدخل هو، وقال أبو يوسف: إنه يقطع؛ لأنه أخرج المال - من
And if he hands it to another: 'another' can be in the nominative, meaning another man took from the entering thief from outside the house, or in the accusative, meaning the thief gave the stolen item to another man. This condition includes four issues as well, and the consequence of all is his statement: 'no', i.e., he is not cut off in all these forms. First: the burglar breaks into the house, enters, takes the goods, and hands it to the other outside the house, so neither is cut off. As for the hander, because he did not effect removal due to an intervening valid hand on the property before its exit. As for the outside man, because he did not effect the breach of the hirz. So the theft is not complete from each one. According to Abu Yusuf, the inside person is cut off in any case. As for the outside person, if he inserted his hand and took the goods from the inside person, he is cut off; if the inside person extended his hand and gave it to the outside, there is no cut on the outside. This is based on the burglar breaking through the wall and inserting his hand—that is the second issue, and al-Shafi'i said the same. Second: he inserted his hand into a house through a breach and took something—he is not cut off, based on the narration from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The thief, if he is clever, is not cut off.' He was asked, 'How is that?' He said: 'He breaks into the house, inserts his hand, and takes out the goods without entering himself.' Abu Yusuf said: he is cut off, because he removed the goods—
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage discusses four situations where the thief hands stolen property to another person outside the hirz, and the ruling that neither is subject to amputation. The core reason is that the complete act of theft (breaking the hirz and removing the property) is not performed by a single person. The inside person (the burglar) did complete the breach, but the property is not removed from the hirz in his hands because another hand (the receiver) intercepts it while it is still conceptually within the hirz. Conversely, the receiver did not break the hirz. The text then references the difference between Abu Yusuf and the general position: Abu Yusuf holds that the inside person is always cut off because he initiated the removal. The outside person's liability depends on whether he actively took the property or merely received it. The passage then transitions to a related second issue: if a thief does not enter the house but only inserts his hand through a hole and takes something, there is disagreement. A hadith from Ali (r) suggests no amputation for such a 'clever' thief, but Abu Yusuf dissents, arguing that removal of the property from the hirz is still accomplished by the thief's hand. The text ends abruptly mid-sentence.

Key Terms
مناولة (munawalah): Handing over; the act of the thief giving the stolen property to another person from outside the hirz.
نقب (naqb): A breach or hole made in a wall. The thief may use it to insert his hand or enter partially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first scenario in this passage, if a thief breaks into a house, takes an item, and hands it to an accomplice waiting outside, what is the general Hanafi ruling?
A) Both are amputated because they conspired.
B) Only the inside thief is amputated.
C) Only the outside accomplice is amputated.
D) Neither is amputated.

Key Takeaway
The hadd penalty for theft requires both breach of the hirz and complete removal by the same person; splitting these acts between two parties exempts both from amputation in the general Hanafi position.

Theft from a House: Four Scenarios and the Hadd Punishment
Rulings on four theft scenarios with proof and internal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that theft is complete when the stolen item exits the hirz, and identify which of the four scenarios result in amputation.

Core Idea
Theft is completed upon removal from hirz; method of removal (even trickery) does not prevent the hadd.

The Hanafi school imposes the hadd of amputation for theft when the stolen property is removed from its secure place (hirz). This lesson examines four common scenarios from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: moving goods between rooms, stealing from a compartment, using a hole to throw goods out, and loading goods onto an animal. Each scenario is judged by whether the item left the hirz, regardless of the thief's tactics. A key internal dispute with Zufar highlights the importance of direct removal by the thief.

Guided Path
1
Define hirz and its role in theft
Identify the concept of hirz (secure storage) as the condition for the hadd of theft. Understand that the stolen item must exit the hirz for the punishment to apply.
Hirz varies with property type and location; here it is the room or compartment.
2
Analyze the four scenarios
Walk through each scenario: (1) moving item from one room to another; (2) entering a maqsura and stealing; (3) making a hole, throwing item out, then retrieving; (4) loading onto a donkey and driving out. For each, determine if the item left the hirz.
All four result in amputation because the item exited the hirz, even if still on the property.
3
Examine the Zufar dispute
Zufar argues that throwing the item out and retrieving later is not theft because if another took it, they would not be cut. Contrast the Hanafi response: the thief himself removed it; the throwing is a trick.
Thamarah al-khilaf: In scenario 3, Hanafi cuts; Zufar does not.
4
Apply the ruling to practical cases
Consider a small house vs. large house. In a small house, all rooms share one hirz; a resident stealing from another room is not cut. In a large house, each room is a separate hirz.
This is detailed in the commentary about large houses being like neighborhoods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من حجرة إلى الدار، أو أغار من أهل الحجر حجرةً، أو نقب فدخل وألقى شيئاً إلى صحنها أي أخذ شخص بسرعة السارق البيت الطريق، ثم أخذه أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه قطع، وإن ناول آخر . الحمار جواب المسائل الأربعة كلها في البيت غلقاً على حدة، والمقصورة مكان يستغني به أهله عن الانتفاع بصحن الدار، والثانية: أغار من أهل المقاصير من على مقصورة وسرق منها شيئاً قطع؛ لأن كل حجرة حرز على حدة، والمراد بالإغارة الإسراع من الأخذ "أغار الثعلب والفرس إغارة" إذا أسرع في العدو، وهذا أيضاً فيما إذا كانت الدار كبيرة؛ لأنها بمنزلة المحلة، بخلاف ما إذا كانت صغيرة، فإن حكمها حكم مكان واحد، فلا يقطع الساكن ولا المأذون له بالدخول فيها إذا سرق من بعض مقاصيرها، والثالثة: إذا نقب اللص البيت فأخذ شيئاً من متاعه، فألقاه في الطريق ثم خرج ثم أخذه، يقطع عندنا، وقال زفر : لا يقطع؛ لأنه إن رماه وتركه وأخذه غيره لا يقطع الآخذ. فكذا إذا أخذه بنفسه، ولنا أنه يخرج من الحرز بنفسه فتمت السرقة والرمي حيلة يعتاده السراق؛ لتعذر الخروج مع المتاع أو ليتفرغ لدفع صاحب الدار أو للفرار، بخلاف ما إذا تركه؛ لأنه مضيع لا سارق.
From one room to the house, or he rushed from the people of one room to another room, or he made a hole and entered and threw something into its courtyard, meaning a person quickly took the thief's house the road, then he took it or carried it on a donkey and drove it and took it out, he is cut. And if he handed over to another... the donkey answer to all four questions in the house each closure separately. The compartment (maqsura) is a place whose residents can dispense with using the courtyard of the house. The second: he rushed from the people of compartments to a compartment and stole something from it, he is cut; because each room is a separate hirz. The meaning of rushing (ighara) is to be quick in taking: 'the fox and the horse rushed' when they speed in running. This also applies when the house is large, because it is like a neighborhood, unlike if it is small, then its ruling is like a single place, so the resident or the one permitted to enter it is not cut if he steals from some of its compartments. The third: if the thief made a hole in the house and took some of its furniture, then threw it into the road, then went out and took it, he is cut according to us. Zufar said: He is not cut; because if he threw it and left it and someone else took it, the taker would not be cut. Likewise if he himself took it. Our proof: He himself removed it from the hirz, so the theft is completed. Throwing is a trick practiced by thieves due to difficulty of exiting with the property, or to be free to repel the owner, or to flee. Unlike if he left it, because he is a waster, not a thief.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage discusses four scenarios related to theft from a house and whether the thief's hand is cut (amputation as prescribed punishment). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists four cases: (1) moving stolen goods from one room to another within the same house, (2) entering a compartment (maqsura) and stealing from it, (3) making a hole, entering, throwing the item into the road, then exiting and retrieving it, (4) loading stolen goods onto a donkey and driving it out. The commentator explains that in all four cases, the hand is cut because the stolen item has exited the hirz (secure place). The author then elaborates on the conditions: a large house is like a neighborhood where each room is a separate hirz; a small house is like a single place, so the resident does not have his hand cut if he steals from one room to another. The third scenario involves a dispute with Imam Zufar, who argues that throwing the item out and then retrieving it does not constitute theft because if someone else took it, that person wouldn't be cut. The Hanafi response is that the thief himself removed the item from the hirz; the throwing is merely a trick. The lesson demonstrates that the punishment hinges on the item's removal from the hirz, not on how the thief physically carries it out.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is stored; the condition for theft punishment.
إغارة (ighara): Rushing quickly to take something.
مقصوره (maqsura): A private compartment or room within a house that is independently secure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
