Diyah and Kaffarah for Musta'min Killing Musta'min
Ruling with proof on diyah and kaffarah for musta'min killing musta'min

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah and kaffarah when a Muslim musta'min kills another in Dar al-Harb, and explain the legal reasoning for why diyah is paid from the killer's own wealth.

Core Idea
Diyah is wajib in both intentional and mistaken killing; qisas is impossible in Dar al-Harb, but isma remains intact.

When two Muslims enter enemy territory with aman and one kills the other, a unique situation arises: qisas cannot be enforced due to lack of Muslim authority, but the victim's inviolability (isma) is not lost. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling that diyah must be paid by the killer in all cases, and explores why the 'aqila does not bear the payment – shedding light on the intersection of penal law and geopolitical context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two Muslim musta'min (holders of aman) enter Dar al-Harb, and one kills the other. The killing could be intentional ('amd) or mistaken (khat').
Understand the factual setting: outside Muslim jurisdiction, no Islamic court or enforcement.
2
State the ruling for diyah
In both intentional and mistaken killing, the killer must pay diyah (blood money) from his own wealth, over three years.
This is a departure from the normal rule where intentional killing would require qisas, not diyah, and mistaken killing would be paid by the 'aqila.
3
Understand why qisas is not applied
Qisas requires the authority and power of the Muslim state (Imam and community). In Dar al-Harb, that is absent, so qisas is impossible. The alternative is diyah.
The impossibility of enforcement (ta'dhur al-istifa') triggers a switch to financial liability.
4
Analyze the basis for diyah: isma remains
The victim's isma (inviolability) is established by Islam and is not lost by entering Dar al-Harb with aman. Therefore, the killing is a crime requiring compensation.
Isma is perpetual for a Muslim; only apostasy removes it.
5
Explain why diyah is from the killer's own wealth
For intentional killing: the 'aqila never bears diyah for 'amd. For mistaken killing: normally the 'aqila pays, but here they cannot protect the killer (due to distance and different jurisdictions), so the killer bears it himself.
The inability to protect (la qudrah bihim 'ala al-siyanah) transfers the obligation to the killer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلمان مستأمنان قتل أحدهما صاحبه تجب الدية في ماله والكفارة في الخطأ سواء كان عمدا أو خطأ وتجب الكفارة أيضاً دون العمد أي إذا دخل المسلمان دار الحرب بأمان فقتل أحدهما صاحبه عمداً أو خطأ فعلى القاتل الدية في ماله في ثلاث سنين في العمد والخطأ والكفارة أيضاً في الخطأ أما الدية فلأنه لم تجب القصاص وقت القتل لتعذر الاستيفاء لأنه إنما يكون بالمنعة ولا منعة دون الإمام وجماعة المسلمين ولم يوجد ذلك في دار الحرب فتجب الدية لأن العصمة لا تبطل بالدخول في دار الحرب لأنها ثابتة بالإسلام فلا تبطل بعارض الدخول بالأمان لا بالكفر وأما وجوبها في ماله في صورة العمد فلأن العاقلة لا تعقل العمد لما عرف في موضعه وأما في صورة الخطأ فلأنه لا قدرة بهم على الصيانة مع تباين الدارين
Two Muslim musta'min, one kills his companion; diyah is obligatory in his wealth, and kaffarah in case of error (khat'), whether it was intentional or mistaken. Kaffarah is also obligatory, but not in intentional killing. (Commentary) i.e., if two Muslims entered Dar al-Harb with aman, and one kills his companion intentionally or by mistake, then the killer must pay the diyah from his own wealth over three years, both in intentional and mistaken killing, and also kaffarah in mistaken killing. As for diyah, it is because qisas is not obligatory at the time of killing, due to difficulty of execution, because it requires the power of enforcement, and there is no power without the Imam and the Muslim community, and that was not present in Dar al-Harb, so diyah is obligatory because inviolability (isma) is not nullified by entering Dar al-Harb; it is established by Islam, so it is not nullified by the incidental entry with aman, not by disbelief. As for its obligation in his wealth in the case of intentional killing, it is because the 'aqila (tribal surety) does not bear intentional killing, as known in its place. As for the case of mistake, it is because they have no ability to protect (the killer) due to the difference of the two lands.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage rules on the situation where two Muslim men enter Dar al-Harb with aman, and one kills the other. The ruling distinguishes between intentional (‘amd) and mistaken (khat’) killing. In both cases, the killer must pay the diyah (blood money) from his own wealth, over three years. The reason qisas (retaliation) is not applied is because its execution requires the authority and power of the Muslim state (the Imam and community), which is not available in Dar al-Harb. Since the victim’s inviolability (isma) remains intact due to his Islam—it is not lost by entering enemy territory—the killer must pay diyah. For intentional killing, the ‘aqila (the group responsible for paying diyah on behalf of the killer) does not bear the diyah because ‘aqila only covers accidental killing. Therefore, the killer pays from his own wealth. For mistaken killing, although normally the ‘aqila would pay, here they cannot because the killer is in Dar al-Harb and the ‘aqila cannot protect or assist him due to the separation of lands (tabayun al-darayn). So the killer pays from his own wealth as well. Additionally, kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory in mistaken killing (freeing a slave or fasting) but not in intentional killing (because intentional killing requires qisas or diyah, not kaffarah). The commentary also notes that in intentional killing, kaffarah is not obligatory; the text says 'kaffarah also in mistake' implying it is not in intentional.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money payable for homicide or injury; here it is from the killer's own wealth.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for accidental killing: freeing a slave or fasting two months.
عصمة (isma): Inviolability of life and honor; established by Islam, not lost by entering Dar al-Harb under aman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require the killer to pay diyah from his own wealth even when the killing is mistaken?
A) Because the 'aqila is not present in Dar al-Harb and cannot protect the killer
B) Because the victim's isma is lost in Dar al-Harb
C) Because the killer acted treacherously
D) Because the 'aqila refuses to pay

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's inviolability (isma) is not lost by entering Dar al-Harb with aman; therefore, diyah is wajib for killing, but enforced from the killer's own wealth due to practical constraints.

The Musta'min's Property After Returning to Dar al-Harb: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States that a musta'min returning to Dar al-Harb loses his aman; his property becomes fay' if he is killed/captured after victory, otherwise to heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a musta'min's property when he returns to Dar al-Harb and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding deposits and pledges.

Core Idea
A musta'min's property becomes fay' only if captured/killed after Muslim victory; otherwise it goes to heirs.

In this lesson, we examine a detailed passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that clarifies the fate of a musta'min's assets (deposits, debts, pledges) when he revokes his aman by returning to enemy territory. The text presents the general rule and then highlights a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. You will learn the conditions under which property becomes fay' (booty) and how this dispute affects practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Musta'min's Status
Determine what happens to the musta'min's person and property when he returns to Dar al-Harb. The text states his blood becomes lawful but his property remains protected until certain events occur.
The aman is nullified for his person immediately, but for property it remains on the verge of loss.
2
Distinguish the Three Scenarios
The passage lists three scenarios for the property becoming fay' (capture after victory, killing after victory, capture without total victory) and one scenario (natural death or killing without victory) where heirs inherit.
Identify the condition of 'victory' as key.
3
Analyze the Disagreement on Deposits
The main text says the deposit becomes fay', but Abu Yusuf holds it becomes the depositary's property because his hand is prior. Muhammad (the author's preference) says it follows the owner and becomes fay'.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different weights given to physical vs. legal possession.
4
Understand the Ruling on Pledges
A similar disagreement occurs: Abu Yusuf says the pledge-taker takes his debt from the pledge; Muhammad says it is sold, debt paid, surplus to treasury.
The thamarah al-khilaf: who benefits from the surplus?
5
Evaluate the Preferred View
The text aligns with Muhammad's position as the general rule (property follows the person). This is likely the mufta bihi view, though Abu Yusuf's opinion is noted.
Later Hanafi fatwa often relies on Muhammad's view in matters of booty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن رجع إليهم وله وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي أو دين عليهما، حل دمه، فإن أسر أو ظهر عليهم، فقتل، سقط دينه، وصارت وديعته فيئا، وإن قُتِل ولم يُظهر عليهم، أو مات، فقرضه ووديعته لورثته. [ أي إذا دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان ثم عاد إلى دار الحرب، وترك وديعته عند مسلم أو ذمي، أو ديناً في ذمتهما، فقد صار دمه مباحاً بالعود إلى دار الحرب؛ لأنه أبطل أمانه، وما كان من ماله في أيدي المسلمين أو الذميين، فهو باق على ما كان عليه حرام التناول؛ لأن حكم أمانه لم يبطل في حق ماله، إلا أنه على شرف الزوال، فإن أسر الحربي المذكور، أو غلبنا على دارهم فقتل سقطت ديونه وصارت الوديعة فيئاً، أما سقوط الدين؛ فلأن إثبات اليد عليه بواسطة المطالبة وقد بطلت ويد من عليه الدين أسبق إليه من يد العامة، فيختص به، ويسقط منه، وأما الوديعة؛ فلأنها في يده تقديراً؛ لأن يد المودع كيده، فيصير فيئاً تبعاً لنفسه، كما إذا كانت في يده حقيقة، وعند أبي يوسف: إنها تصير ملكاً للمودع؛ لأن يده فيها أسبق فكان بها أحق، ولو كان له رهناً فعند أبي يوسف: يأخذ المرتمن بدينه، وقال محمد: يباع ويوفى بثمنه الدين، والفاضل لبيت المال. ثم اعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف من سقوط دينه وصيرورة وديعته فيئاً في ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن يظهر المسلمون على دار الحرب ويأخذوا الراجع، الثاني: أن يظهروا ويقتلوه، الثالث: أن يأخذوه أسيراً من غير غلبة، فقوله: فإن أسر بيان للوجه الثالث، وقوله : أو ظهر عليه بيان للأولين؛ لأنه أعم من أن يقتلوه أو لا لكنه شامل لما إذا صاروا مغلوبين وهرب هو فإن ماله يبقى له فلابد من تقييد الظهور عليهم بأن يأخذوه أو يقتلوه. (عيني، فتح) وإن قتل: أي الحربي المذكور والحال أنه لم يغلب على الذين هو فيهم. (عيني) ولم يظهر عليهم أي فإن قتل هذا المستأمن من غير أن يظهر عليهم، فدينه الذي على المسلم أو الذمي أو وديعته التي عندهما لورثته، وكذا لو مات حتف أنفه؛ لأن نفسه لما لم يصر مغنومة فكذا المال لا يكون مغنوماً.
If he returns to them and he has a deposit with a Muslim or a dhimmi, or a debt owed by them, his blood becomes lawful. If he is captured or they are defeated and he is killed, his debt drops and his deposit becomes fay'. And if he is killed without them being defeated, or he dies, his loan and deposit go to his heirs. [If a harbi enters our land with aman then returns to Dar al-Harb, leaving a deposit with a Muslim or dhimmi, or a debt owed by them, his blood becomes permissible by returning to Dar al-Harb because he has nullified his aman. His property in the hands of Muslims or dhimmis remains forbidden to take because his aman is not nullified regarding his property, but it is on the verge of being lost. If he is captured or we overcome them and he is killed, his debts drop and the deposit becomes fay'. As for the debt drop: because the ability to claim it is lost, and the debtor's hand is prior to the public's hand, so he keeps it and it drops from him. As for the deposit: because it is in his hand in a legal sense (the depositary's hand is like his hand), so it becomes fay' following himself, as if it were actually in his hand. Abu Yusuf says: It becomes ownership of the depositary because his hand was prior. If he had a pledge, Abu Yusuf says: the pledge-taker takes his due; Muhammad says: it is sold and the debt paid from its price, the surplus goes to the treasury. Then know that what the author mentioned of the drop of debt and deposit becoming fay' covers three scenarios: 1) Muslims overcome Dar al-Harb and take him, 2) they overcome and kill him, 3) they capture him without overcoming. 'If he is captured' explains the third; 'or they are defeated' covers the first two, but it must be qualified that they actually take or kill him. If he is killed without them being defeated, or dies naturally, his debt and deposit go to his heirs because his life was not taken as booty, so neither is his property.]
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the fate of a musta'min's property when he returns to Dar al-Harb. The text first states the general rule: by returning, he nullifies his aman, becoming an enemy (halal al-dam). However, his property in Muslim or dhimmi hands is not immediately seized because his aman's effect on property remains until something happens. The key point: if he is captured or killed by Muslims after they defeat his party, his debts are dropped (the debtor is freed) and his deposit becomes fay' (booty for Muslims). But if he is killed without the Muslims prevailing (e.g., in a battle they lose, or he dies naturally), his property goes to his heirs. There is also a disagreement: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if the musta'min leaves a deposit, upon his capture/killing it becomes the property of the depositary (the Muslim or dhimmi holding it) because his hand was prior. Imam Muhammad holds that it becomes fay' (for the state) after paying any debts. The text also addresses the case of a pledge: Abu Yusuf says the pledge-taker takes his debt; Muhammad says it is sold and the debt paid, with surplus to the treasury. The commentary concludes by clarifying the three scenarios for the drop of debt and deposit becoming fay': (1) Muslims take him alive after victory, (2) they kill him after victory, (3) they capture him without total victory. In each case, the property is taken. But if he is killed in a losing battle, his heirs inherit. The reasoning is that his property follows his fate: if his life becomes booty (by capture or killing after victory), so does his property; if not, it remains for heirs.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Booty taken from enemies without battle or after they are defeated; here specifically property of the musta'min that becomes state property.
وديعة (wadi'a): A deposit left with someone for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens to a musta'min's deposit if he is captured alive after Muslim victory over his group?
A) It goes to his heirs.
B) It becomes fay' (state property).
C) It remains with the depositary permanently.
D) It is destroyed.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min's property in Dar al-Islam only becomes fay' if he is captured or killed after Muslim victory; otherwise it goes to his heirs, and within the Hanafi school there is a valid disagreement on whether the depositary acquires ownership.

The Effect of Marriage on a Female Musta'mina's Status
Legal ruling on gender-asymmetric status change upon marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the status change of a female musta'mina who marries a dhimmi and distinguish it from the case of a male musta'min.

Core Idea
A female musta'mina becomes a dhimmi by marriage; a male musta'min does not.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi chapter on Siyar concerning the effect of marriage on the status of a musta'min (a non-Muslim temporarily protected in dar al-Islam). The text presents an asymmetric rule: a female musta'mina who marries a dhimmi becomes a permanent resident (dhimmi), while a male musta'min who marries a dhimmi woman retains his temporary status. Understanding the rationale behind this distinction is essential for correctly applying the principles of aman and dhimma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Separate the ruling into two cases: a female musta'mina marrying a dhimmi, and a male musta'min marrying a dhimmi woman.
The text explicitly contrasts the two.
2
State the ruling for each case
For the female: she becomes a dhimmi. For the male: he does not become a dhimmi.
The ruling is categorical.
3
Explain the rationale (ta'lil)
The female becomes a dhimmi because she commits to permanent residence following her husband (taba'iyya). The male does not because he can divorce and return to dar al-harb before his aman expires.
The rationale is based on the ability to leave.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
The female loses her right to return to dar al-harb; the male retains that right.
This affects the individual's legal capacity to travel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما بقي من أحكام المستأمن أو نكحت ذمياً لا عكسه. حنف أنفه ولا يصير فينا فيرد عليهم كما يرد في حياة أو نكحت ذمياً: أي إذا دخلت حربية دارنا بأمان فتزوجت ذمياً صارت ذمية، ولم تترك أن ترجع إلى دار الحرب؛ لأنها التزمت المقام في دارنا تبعاً للزوج، وتصير ذمية بمجرد التزوج ههنا؛ لأنها تابعة للرجل في السكنى. لا عكسه أي إذا دخل الحربي بأمان في دارنا فتزوج ذمية لا يصير ذمياً؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يطلقها ويرجع إلى دارهم قبل انقضاء المدة فلم يكن ملتزماً للمقام في دارنا فله أن يرجع إن شاء.(مستخلص،عيني)
Remaining rulings of the musta'min: Or if she married a dhimmi, not the opposite. [Commentary]: 'Or if she married a dhimmi': meaning if a female Harbi enters our land with aman and marries a dhimmi, she becomes a dhimmi and is not allowed to return to the war land; because she has committed to staying in our land following the husband, and she becomes a dhimmi merely by marriage here; because she follows the man in residence. 'Not the opposite': meaning if a male Harbi enters our land with aman and marries a dhimmi woman, he does not become a dhimmi; because he can divorce her and return to their land before the end of the period, so he has not committed to staying in our land, so he may return if he wishes.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling (hukm) from the chapter on Siyar concerning the status of a musta'min (protected person) who marries a dhimmi. The ruling differentiates based on gender. A female musta'mina who marries a dhimmi becomes a dhimmi herself and forfeits her right to return to dar al-harb. The reason is that by marriage she commits to permanent residence in dar al-Islam following her husband. Conversely, a male musta'min who marries a dhimmi woman does not become a dhimmi because he retains the option to divorce and return to dar al-harb before his aman expires. The commentary clarifies that the female's status changes immediately upon the marriage contract due to her dependence on her husband for residence.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person from dar al-harb who has been granted aman (safe conduct) to enter dar al-Islam temporarily.
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim permanent resident of dar al-Islam who enjoys the protection (dhimma) of the Muslim state.
تبعية (taba'iyya): The principle of following the husband in residence; the wife's legal status is tied to her husband's domicile.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a female musta'mina (protected person) marries a dhimmi man in dar al-Islam?
A) She remains a musta'mina and can return to dar al-harb at any time.
B) She becomes a dhimmi and cannot return to dar al-harb.
C) She becomes a Muslim and must give up her aman.
D) Her status is unchanged until the aman period expires.

Key Takeaway
A female musta'mina becomes a dhimmi by marrying a dhimmi; a male musta'min does not, because he retains the option to divorce and return to dar al-harb.

Conversion of a Musta'min: Fate of Family and Property
Ruling on status of family and property of a musta'min convert

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two scenarios of a musta'min's conversion and state the Hanafi ruling on whether his wife, children, and property become fay' or remain protected.

Core Idea
A musta'min's conversion location determines his minor children's freedom and property protection.

This lesson examines a classic Siyar passage that addresses what happens when a musta'min (a person who entered dar al-Islam with aman) converts to Islam while his family and property remain in dar al-harb. The Hanafi school draws a crucial distinction based on where the conversion takes place—inside dar al-Islam or inside dar al-harb. This distinction determines whether the convert's minor children follow him as free Muslims or become part of the spoils (fay'), and whether his deposited property remains protected. The passage also delves into the legal reasoning involving the concept of 'isma (inviolability) and the authority of the depositee's hand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) the musta'min converts in dar al-Islam, then Muslims overcome dar al-harb; (2) the musta'min converts in dar al-harb, then comes to dar al-Islam, and Muslims subsequently overcome dar al-harb. Notice the difference in where the conversion occurs.
These scenarios are often labeled 'aslama huna' vs 'aslama thumma'.
2
Analyze the ruling on family members
In the first scenario, the wife, adult children, and even minor children become fay' (spoils) because they are not under the father's authority at the time of conversion (different residence). In the second scenario, minor children are free and Muslim because they were under his guardianship when he converted in dar al-harb (same residence). The wife and adult children remain Harbis and are fay'.
The key principle is ittihad al-dar (unity of residence) for the father's Islam to extend to his dependents.
3
Trace the reasoning for property
Property deposited with a Muslim or dhimmi in dar al-Islam is protected because the deposit was made in a land of protection ('isma). If the convert dies or is killed, the property goes to his heirs. In contrast, property in dar al-harb or in the hand of a Harbi becomes fay' because the hand of a Harbi is not respected, and the owner's own person being spoilable makes the property follow.
The hand of the depositee in dar al-Islam is considered like the owner's hand for protection purposes.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The passage references the Prophet's hadith 'They have protected from me their blood and their wealth' (عصموا مني دمائهم وأموالهم). The commentary explains that this is not absolute—it applies only to property in the owner's actual possession or equivalent. Property in dar al-harb is not covered by that protection when the owner becomes spoilable.
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim: 'أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله... عصموا مني دمائهم وأموالهم'.
5
Apply the practical outcome
The conclusion: if the convert is alive after the conquest, he takes his protected property; if he died, his heirs take it. In the first scenario, even minor children are fay', so they become part of the spoils. In the second scenario, minor children are free and deposits are protected, but wife, adult children, and property with Harbis are fay'.
This ruling demonstrates the nuanced application of siyar principles in military contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جاءنا حربي بأمان وله زوجة عمه، وولد ومال عند مسلم وذمي أو حربي، أي في دار الحرب فأسلم هنا، ثم ظهر عليهم، فالكل فيء، وإن أسلم ثمه، فجاءنا فظهر عليهم، فولده في دار الإسلام الصغير حرّ مسلم، وما أودعه عند مسلم أو ذمي تبعا له أي المال الذي خرج إلى دار الإسلام فيرد على ورثته؛ لأنهم قائمون مقامه؛ وهذا لأن العصمة كانت ثابتة وقت الإيداع؛ لأن دار الإسلام دار العصمة، فلما لم يظهر على دار الحرب، كانت العصمة باقية كما كانت فكان حكم الأمان باقياً في ماله، فيرد على ورثته بعد موته أو قتله؛ لأنه صار كأنه مات والمال في يده، بخلاف المسألة الأولى؛ لأن نفسه لما كانت مغنومة كان ماله كذلك، فيكون فيئاً بالتبعية، ولا يكون يد المودع كيده؛ لأن دار الحرب ليس بدار عصمة، ويد المودع كيده من وجه دون وجه فلا يكون وديعة معصومة بالشك، والحاصل أن في الوجهين يبقى ماله على حاله، فيأخذه إن كان حيّاً، أو ورثته إن مات، الأول: أن يظهروا على الدار، فيهرب هو، والثاني: أن يقتلوه، ولم يظهروا على الدار، أو يموت ولو غير بدل قرضه الدين لكان أولى؛ ليشتمل سائر الديون. (مستخلص، فتح عيني) وله زوجة أي والحال أنه قد كانت له.(ط، عيني) وولد سواء كان صغيرا أو كبيرا. (عيني) ظهر عليهم أي على أهل دار الحرب الذي هو منهم. (عيني) فالكل فيء أي لو دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان وله امرأة في دار الحرب، وأولاد صغار وكبار، ومال أودع بعضه عند مسلم، وبعضه عند ذمي، وبعضه عند حربي، فأسلم الحربي في دار الإسلام ثم ظهر على الدار، فذلك كله فيء، أما المرأة وأولاده الكبار؛ فلأنهم حربيون كبار، وليسوا بأتباع، وكذلك ما في بطن الزوجة لو كانت حاملاً؛ لأنه جزؤها، وأما أولاده الصغار؛ فلأن شرط التبعية في الإسلام اتحاد الدار وهو مفقود في هذه الصورة، فلو الصبي في هذه المسألة وصار في دارنا فهو مسلم تبعاً لأبيه مع بقاء كونه فيئاً، وأما أمواله؛ فلأنها لا تصير محرزة بإحراز نفسه لاختلاف الدار، وقوله علية: "عصموا مني دمائهم وأموالهم ليس على إطلاقه، بل بالنسبة للمال الذي في يده، وما في معناه، فبقي الأموال فيئاً وغنيمة. (مستخلص، فتح) سبی وإن أسلم ثمه إلخ المسألة الأولى كان على تقدير أن الحربي المذكور الداخل بأمان عندنا أسلم في دار الإسلام، وهذه على تقدير أنه أسلم في دار الحرب، أي إن أسلم فيها ثم خرج إلى دار الإسلام، فظهرنا على الدار، فالحكم في هذه الصورة: أن أولاده الصغار أحرار مسلمون تبعاً لأبيهم؛ لأنهم كانوا تحت ولايته حين أسلم والدار واحدة وما كان له وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي، فهو له؛ لأنه في يد محترمة، ويدهما ،كيده وما سوى ذلك أي المرأة وأولاده الكبار وماله الذي في يد حربي فيء؛ لأن المرأة وأولاده الكبار ليسوا بتابعيه، وأما المال الذي في يد حربي؛ فلأنه لم يصر معصوماً؛ لعدم احترام يد الحربي، وقيد بقوله : "وما أودعه"؛ لأن ما كان في يد المسلم أو الذمي غصباً فهو فيء؛ لعدم النيابة، والمراد بالفيء الغنيمة مجازاً. مستخلص عيني) فظهر عليهم أي على أهل دار الحرب الذين هو منهم. (عيني)
And if a Harbi comes to us with aman and he has a wife (lit. wife of his uncle), child, and property with a Muslim, dhimmi, or Harbi, i.e., in dar al-harb, and he becomes Muslim here, then we overcome them, then everything is fay'. And if he becomes Muslim there, then he comes to us and we overcome them, then his child in dar al-Islam, the minor, is free and Muslim, and what he deposited with a Muslim or dhimmi follows him – meaning the property that went out to dar al-Islam – so it is given to his heirs; because they stand in his place; and this is because protection (isma) was established at the time of deposit, because dar al-Islam is the land of protection. Since he did not overcome dar al-harb, the protection remained as it was, so the ruling of his aman remained on his property, so it is given to his heirs after his death or killing; because it is as if he died with the property in his hand, contrary to the first issue; because when his self was made spoils, his property likewise becomes fay' by following, and the hand of the depositee is not like his hand; because dar al-harb is not the land of protection, and the hand of the depositee is like his hand in one aspect but not another, so the deposit is not protected by doubt. The conclusion is that in two aspects his property remains as it is, he takes it if alive, or his heirs if he dies: first: they overcome the land and he flees; second: they kill him without overcoming the land, or he dies. And if instead of a loan, the debt would be more appropriate to include all debts. (Mukhtalaf, Fath Ayni) 'And he has a wife' meaning that he had one. (T, Ayni) 'Child' whether minor or major. (Ayni) 'Overcome them' meaning the inhabitants of dar al-harb from which he is. (Ayni) 'Then everything is fay' meaning: if a Harbi enters our land with aman, having a wife in dar al-harb, minor and adult children, and property deposited partly with a Muslim, partly with a dhimmi, partly with a Harbi, and he becomes Muslim in dar al-Islam, then we overcome the land, then all of that is fay'. As for the wife and adult children, because they are adult Harbis, not followers, and likewise what is in the wife's womb if she is pregnant, because it is a part of her. As for his minor children, because the condition of following in Islam is unity of residence (ittihad al-dar), which is lacking in this scenario. So even if the child in this issue comes to our land, he is Muslim following his father, while remaining fay'. As for his property, because it does not become preserved (muhraz) by the preservation of his self due to the difference of residence. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'They have protected from me their blood and their wealth' is not absolute, but rather regarding the property in his hand and what is in its meaning. So the properties remain fay' and booty. (Mukhtalaf, Fath) 'And if he becomes Muslim there etc.' The first issue was on the assumption that the mentioned Harbi who entered with aman among us became Muslim in dar al-Islam, and this one is on the assumption that he became Muslim in dar al-harb, meaning he became Muslim therein, then came out to dar al-Islam, and we overcame the land. The ruling in this scenario: his minor children are free Muslims following their father, because they were under his guardianship when he became Muslim and the residence is one. What he had deposited with a Muslim or dhimmi is his, because it is in a protected hand, and their hand is like his hand. What is other than that, i.e., the wife, adult children, and his property in the hand of a Harbi, is fay', because the wife and adult children are not his followers. As for the property in the hand of a Harbi, because it did not become protected due to the lack of respect for the hand of a Harbi. And he restricted it by saying 'what he deposited' because what was in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi as usurpation (ghasb) is fay' due to lack of agency. And the intended meaning of fay' is booty metaphorically. (Mukhtalaf, Ayni) 'They overcame them' meaning the inhabitants of dar al-harb from which he is. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings (ahkam) from the Siyar chapter, addressing the scenario where a musta'min enters dar al-Islam with aman while his family and property remain in dar al-harb, and then he converts to Islam. Two scenarios are distinguished: (1) If he converts in dar al-Islam, then Muslims overcome dar al-harb, everything (his wife, children, and property) becomes fay' (spoils). The wife and adult children are considered Harbis who are not his followers, and minor children are fay' because the unity of residence (ittihad al-dar) required for them to follow him is not met. (2) If he converts in dar al-harb (while still there), then later comes to dar al-Islam, and Muslims overcome the land, the ruling differs: his minor children are free and Muslim, following him, because they were under his authority when he converted and the residence (dar al-harb) was the same; his deposits with Muslims or dhimmis remain his property; but his wife, adult children, and property in Harbi hands become fay'. The reasoning involves the concept of 'isma (inviolability) – property deposited in dar al-Islam is protected because it was placed in a trust environment; once the owner becomes spoilable, the property follows. The commentary cites a hadith 'They have protected from me their blood and their wealth' and explains it is not absolute; it applies only to property in the owner's actual possession or equivalent. The passage notes that the hand of a depositee in dar al-harb is not fully equivalent to the owner's hand due to lack of protection.

Key Terms
أمان (amān): Safe-conduct or guarantee of protection granted to a Harbi allowing temporary entry into dar al-Islam.
فيء (fay'): Spoils taken from non-Muslims without active fighting; in this passage used interchangeably with ghanimah (booty).
عصمة (ʿiṣma): Inviolability or legal protection of life and property.
اتحاد الدار (ittiḥād al-dār): Unity of residence; the condition that the parent and child must be in the same territory for the child to follow the parent's conversion.
مستأمن (musta'min): A Harbi who has been granted aman and is temporarily residing in dar al-Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a musta'min converts to Islam while in dar al-Islam, what is the status of his minor children still in dar al-harb after Muslims conquer that land?
A) They become free and Muslim following his conversion.
B) They become fay' (spoils) for the Muslims.
C) They retain their musta'min status.
D) They inherit their father's property.

Key Takeaway
The location of a musta'min's conversion (dar al-Islam vs dar al-harb) critically determines whether his minor children and deposited property remain protected or become spoils of war.

6 min
Property of the Musta'man: Protected Possession vs. Enemy Hand
Property rule and qiyas on hand validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qiyas that determines whether a musta'man's property is protected or becomes fay', based on the validity of the possessing hand (yadd).

Core Idea
A musta'man's property is protected only when in a Muslim or dhimmi's hand; a harbi's hand is not respected.

The passage opens with a key principle in siyar: the inviolability of a musta'man's property depends on who holds it. This lesson unpacks the qiyas that distinguishes a valid, respected hand from an invalid one, and how this determines whether the property remains his or becomes booty (fay').

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contrasting scenarios
The text states that property in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi belongs to the musta'man, but property in the hand of a harbi is fay'.
This opposition sets up the qiyas.
2
State the asl (original case) and its ruling
The asl is: property in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi is protected because that hand is valid and respected (yadd sahiha wa muhtarama).
The ruling: it belongs to the musta'man.
3
State the far' (new case)
The far' is: property in the hand of a harbi.
The harbi is not under Islamic jurisdiction.
4
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is the validity and respect of the hand (sihhat al-yadd wa ihtiramuha). The Muslim/dhimmi's hand is valid; the harbi's hand is not.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted by tahqiq al-manat.
5
Derive the hukm for the far'
Since the harbi's hand lacks validity and respect, the property is not protected and becomes fay' (booty for the Muslim army).
The qiyas is straightforward because the illa is shared.

أي عاقلة القاتل

(عيني) وغيره أي غير ما ذكر من ولده الصغير ووديعته التي عند مسلم أو ذمي وهو زوجته وأولاده الكبار وعقاره ووديعته في يد حربي. (عيني) فيء: أي غنيمة للغانمين؛ لعدم العصمة وعدم التبعية. What remains of the rulings of the musta'man. So it (his property) belongs to him, and other than it is fay' (booty). And whoever kills a Muslim by mistake having no wali, or a harbi who came to us with aman and then became Muslim, his blood money is upon his 'aqila, for the Imam. And in intentional killing, either retaliation or blood money, not pardon. That is: the harbi. That is: the 'aqila of the killer. That is: the obligatory is qisas execution. So 'it belongs to him' means to the harbi who became Muslim; because their hand (possession) is like his own hand, contrary to when it is with a harbi; because his hand is not valid nor respected. (Ayni) 'Other than it' means other than what was mentioned: his minor child and his deposit which is with a Muslim or a dhimmi, namely his wife, adult children, real estate, and deposit in the hand of a harbi. (Ayni) 'Fay'' means spoils for the plunderers; due to lack of inviolability and lack of dependency. (T, Ayni) 'And whoever kills a Muslim by mistake etc.' The issue has four types: First: A Muslim kills a Muslim, while he has no wali, and the killing is by mistake; the ruling is that the blood money is upon his 'aqila, and the right to take it is for the Imam to place it in the public treasury. Second: A Muslim kills a Muslim intentionally, and he has no wali at all, neither present nor absent; the Imam has the choice between inflicting qisas or settling for blood money, and he does not have the right to pardon. Third: A Muslim kills a harbi who had become Muslim after coming to us with aman by mistake; his blood money is upon his 'aqila as before. Fourth: A Muslim kills the harbi after his becoming Muslim intentionally; the Imam has the choice between inflicting qisas or settling for blood money, and this is when the slain has no wali in our abode. And he restricted his statement 'no wali' because if he had a wali, the matter is up to him, not the Imam. And he restricted his statement 'with aman and becoming Muslim' because if the harbi was not a musta'man or did not become Muslim, then killing him carries nothing. If it is said: The musta'man's property cannot be interfered with, so how is his blood not protected by blood money? The answer: Sanctity of interfering with his property and blood does not necessitate that his soul is inviolable (ma'sum); because that sanctity is due to the contract of aman, not because he is inviolable. Al-Zayla'i said: The correct view is that the blood money of the musta'man is like the blood money of a dhimmi. And his statement 'or blood money' means in intentional killing the inevitable punishment is qisas, but taking blood money is permissible because it is more beneficial in this case. [Settlement] by reconciliation and mutual consent; because qisas is [not] better in this case, so he has authority to settle for property. And his statement 'no' 'pardon' means the Imam does not have the right to pardon the killing; because the right belongs to the public and the Imam's authority is advisory, and it is not part of advisability to drop their right without compensation. Similarly, if the slain was a foundling (laqit), then if the killing was by mistake, blood money; if intentional, then the killer is to be killed according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: He does not have qisas; because he is never devoid of an heir usually, or the heir is probable, so there is doubt, and qisas falls by it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): The unknown who cannot be reached is not a wali; because the dead does not benefit from him, so authority transfers to the sultan as in inheritance. (Ayni, Mustakhlas, Fath) 'His blood money' means the blood money of the slain in both cases (Ayni) 'for the Imam' means taking it for the Imam and placing it in the public treasury; because he is appointed as overseer for the Muslims, and this is a matter of oversight. And if there were a wali, the matter would go to him. (T, Ayni) 'And in intentional killing' means intentional killing in the aforementioned case (Ayni) 'or blood money' by way of reconciliation and mutual consent; the Imam looks into it, and whichever he sees as more beneficial he does. (T, Ayni) 'No pardon' means pardon for free is not permissible in the two matters. (T)
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Siyar continues the rulings on the musta'man (a person from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe conduct). It first clarifies that if a musta'man has property or a minor child in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi, that property remains his; otherwise it is fay' (booty for the Muslim army). The reasoning is that the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi is like the musta'man's own hand (possession is respected), whereas a harbi's hand is not. Then the passage presents four cases of killing where the victim has no known wali: (1) a Muslim killed by mistake: blood money on the killer's 'aqila, paid by the Imam to the treasury; (2) a Muslim killed intentionally: Imam may choose qisas or blood money (but not pardon); (3) a musta'man who became Muslim killed by mistake: blood money on the 'aqila; (4) a musta'man who became Muslim killed intentionally: Imam may choose qisas or blood money. The restrictions are explained: 'no wali' because if there is a wali, he decides; 'with aman and Islam' because a pure harbi killed has no liability. An objection is raised: why is the musta'man's blood not protected if his property is? The answer: sanctity comes from the contract of aman, not from personal inviolability (isma). Al-Zayla'i records that the musta'man's blood money equals a dhimmi's. On the foundling (laqit): Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say qisas is possible; Abu Yusuf demurs due to doubt about existence of an heir. The majority hold that the Imam acts as wali for the public interest. The passage concludes that the Imam may not pardon for free.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'man): A person from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe conduct (aman).
أمان (aman): Safe conduct granted to a harbi, making him a musta'man.
فيء (fay'): Booty taken from the enemy without fighting (or in this context, property not protected by aman becomes fay' for the Muslim army).
يَدّ (yadd): Possession or hand; in fiqh, the legal possession that confers protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason a musta'man's property is protected when held by a Muslim?
A) Because the Muslim is trustworthy.
B) Because the Muslim's hand is valid and respected.
C) Because the musta'man owns it.
D) Because it cannot be taken as booty.

Key Takeaway
The musta'man's property is protected when in a valid hand (Muslim/dhimmi) but becomes fay' in an invalid hand (harbi).

Four Killing Cases Involving the Musta'man: The Imam's Authority
Four killing cases and foundling dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four killing scenarios involving a musta'man who became Muslim, and explain the Imam's options (qisas or blood money) and why pardon is not allowed.

Core Idea
The Imam may choose qisas or blood money for intentional killing of a musta'man with no wali, but may not pardon.

This passage from Kitab al-Siyar presents four cases where a musta'man who has embraced Islam is killed. The rules depend on whether the killing is accidental or intentional, and who the victim was. The Imam acts as the public's representative when there is no personal wali. Additionally, an intra-madhhab dispute about the foundling (laqit) illustrates how doubt affects qisas.

Guided Path
1
List the four cases
Identify the four situations: (1) Muslim kills Muslim by mistake, no wali; (2) Muslim kills Muslim intentionally, no wali; (3) Muslim kills musta'man (became Muslim) by mistake; (4) Muslim kills musta'man (became Muslim) intentionally.
The victim must have become Muslim after aman.
2
Identify the common condition
In all cases, the victim has no known wali (blood avenger) in dar al-Islam. This condition triggers the Imam's authority.
If a wali exists, he decides: he may demand qisas, accept blood money, or pardon.
3
Distinguish intentional vs accidental
Accidental killing results only in blood money (diya) on the killer's 'aqila. Intentional killing gives the Imam the choice between qisas (execution) or blood money (by reconciliation).
The text says: 'الواجب هو القتل قصاصا' — the obligatory is qisas, but blood money is permissible if more beneficial.
4
Note the foundling dispute
The text extends the ruling to a foundling (laqit) killed intentionally: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad allow qisas; Abu Yusuf says no qisas due to doubt (shubha) about an unknown heir.
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that an unknown heir is not a wali, so authority transfers to the sultan.
5
Explain why Imam cannot pardon
The Imam's authority is advisory (wilaya nazariyya). He cannot waive the public's right without compensation. Pardon would be eschewal of a public claim for nothing.
This applies to both Muslim victim and musta'man victim.

لا العفو: أي لا يجوز العفو مجانا في المسألتين.(ط)
What remains of the rulings of the musta'man. So it (his property) belongs to him, and other than it is fay' (booty). And whoever kills a Muslim by mistake having no wali, or a harbi who came to us with aman and then became Muslim, his blood money is upon his 'aqila, for the Imam. And in intentional killing, either retaliation or blood money, not pardon. That is: the harbi. That is: the 'aqila of the killer. That is: the obligatory is qisas execution. So 'it belongs to him' means to the harbi who became Muslim; because their hand (possession) is like his own hand, contrary to when it is with a harbi; because his hand is not valid nor respected. (Ayni) 'Other than it' means other than what was mentioned: his minor child and his deposit which is with a Muslim or a dhimmi, namely his wife, adult children, real estate, and deposit in the hand of a harbi. (Ayni) 'Fay'' means spoils for the plunderers; due to lack of inviolability and lack of dependency. (T, Ayni) 'And whoever kills a Muslim by mistake etc.' The issue has four types: First: A Muslim kills a Muslim, while he has no wali, and the killing is by mistake; the ruling is that the blood money is upon his 'aqila, and the right to take it is for the Imam to place it in the public treasury. Second: A Muslim kills a Muslim intentionally, and he has no wali at all, neither present nor absent; the Imam has the choice between inflicting qisas or settling for blood money, and he does not have the right to pardon. Third: A Muslim kills a harbi who had become Muslim after coming to us with aman by mistake; his blood money is upon his 'aqila as before. Fourth: A Muslim kills the harbi after his becoming Muslim intentionally; the Imam has the choice between inflicting qisas or settling for blood money, and this is when the slain has no wali in our abode. And he restricted his statement 'no wali' because if he had a wali, the matter is up to him, not the Imam. And he restricted his statement 'with aman and becoming Muslim' because if the harbi was not a musta'man or did not become Muslim, then killing him carries nothing. If it is said: The musta'man's property cannot be interfered with, so how is his blood not protected by blood money? The answer: Sanctity of interfering with his property and blood does not necessitate that his soul is inviolable (ma'sum); because that sanctity is due to the contract of aman, not because he is inviolable. Al-Zayla'i said: The correct view is that the blood money of the musta'man is like the blood money of a dhimmi. And his statement 'or blood money' means in intentional killing the inevitable punishment is qisas, but taking blood money is permissible because it is more beneficial in this case. [Settlement] by reconciliation and mutual consent; because qisas is [not] better in this case, so he has authority to settle for property. And his statement 'no' 'pardon' means the Imam does not have the right to pardon the killing; because the right belongs to the public and the Imam's authority is advisory, and it is not part of advisability to drop their right without compensation. Similarly, if the slain was a foundling (laqit), then if the killing was by mistake, blood money; if intentional, then the killer is to be killed according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: He does not have qisas; because he is never devoid of an heir usually, or the heir is probable, so there is doubt, and qisas falls by it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): The unknown who cannot be reached is not a wali; because the dead does not benefit from him, so authority transfers to the sultan as in inheritance. (Ayni, Mustakhlas, Fath) 'His blood money' means the blood money of the slain in both cases (Ayni) 'for the Imam' means taking it for the Imam and placing it in the public treasury; because he is appointed as overseer for the Muslims, and this is a matter of oversight. And if there were a wali, the matter would go to him. (T, Ayni) 'And in intentional killing' means intentional killing in the aforementioned case (Ayni) 'or blood money' by way of reconciliation and mutual consent; the Imam looks into it, and whichever he sees as more beneficial he does. (T, Ayni) 'No pardon' means pardon for free is not permissible in the two matters. (T)
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Siyar continues the rulings on the musta'man (a person from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe conduct). It first clarifies that if a musta'man has property or a minor child in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi, that property remains his; otherwise it is fay' (booty for the Muslim army). The reasoning is that the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi is like the musta'man's own hand (possession is respected), whereas a harbi's hand is not. Then the passage presents four cases of killing where the victim has no known wali: (1) a Muslim killed by mistake: blood money on the killer's 'aqila, paid by the Imam to the treasury; (2) a Muslim killed intentionally: Imam may choose qisas or blood money (but not pardon); (3) a musta'man who became Muslim killed by mistake: blood money on the 'aqila; (4) a musta'man who became Muslim killed intentionally: Imam may choose qisas or blood money. The restrictions are explained: 'no wali' because if there is a wali, he decides; 'with aman and Islam' because a pure harbi killed has no liability. An objection is raised: why is the musta'man's blood not protected if his property is? The answer: sanctity comes from the contract of aman, not from personal inviolability (isma). Al-Zayla'i records that the musta'man's blood money equals a dhimmi's. On the foundling (laqit): Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say qisas is possible; Abu Yusuf demurs due to doubt about existence of an heir. The majority hold that the Imam acts as wali for the public interest. The passage concludes that the Imam may not pardon for free.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'man): A person from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe conduct (aman).
أمان (aman): Safe conduct granted to a harbi, making him a musta'man.
عاقلة (aqila): The paternal male relatives of the killer who bear the blood money in cases of accidental killing.
لقيط (laqit): A foundling, an abandoned infant whose lineage is unknown.
ولي (wali): Legal guardian or avenger of blood; the person entitled to demand qisas or accept blood money.
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt that can mitigate a penalty, especially in hudud and qisas cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In case a Muslim intentionally kills a musta'man who had become Muslim, and the slain has no wali, what options does the Imam have?
A) Only qisas
B) Only blood money
C) Either qisas or blood money, but not pardon
D) Pardon without compensation

Key Takeaway
In intentional killing of a musta'man (became Muslim) with no wali, the Imam may choose qisas or blood money, but never pardon.

Definitions of 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya and Land Classification
Defines taxes and classifies lands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of 'ushr, kharaj, and jizya, and classify lands as 'ushri or kharaji according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Land classification: Arab lands are always 'ushri; conquered lands not divided are kharaji.

This passage opens the chapter on 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya. It defines these terms and lays out the Hanafi classification of lands into 'ushri (tithe) and kharaji (land tax). Understanding this classification is crucial because the legal obligations on land and its inhabitants depend entirely on whether the land is 'ushri or kharaji. The source also provides the reasoning behind the classification, rooted in the practice of the Prophet and the early Caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three financial obligations
Locate the definitions of 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya in the passage. Note that 'Ushr is a tithe on produce of 'ushri lands, Kharaj is a land tax on non-Muslims, and Jizya is a poll tax on dhimmis.
'Ushr is treated like zakat (intention required); Kharaj and Jizya are not acts of worship.
2
Classify 'ushri lands
From the passage, list the three categories that make land 'ushri: (1) the land of the Arabs (Arabian Peninsula), (2) any land whose inhabitants embraced Islam, (3) any land conquered by force and divided among the soldiers (ghanimah).
3
Classify kharaji lands
Identify the kharaji categories: (1) the Sawad (Iraq) and any land conquered by force and not divided, (2) any land where the Imam made a peace treaty imposing kharaj.
The Sawad is the prototype; it was conquered by 'Umar and kharaj was imposed with consensus.
4
Understand the reasoning
Note the reason why Arab lands are 'ushri: kharaj requires that the inhabitants be allowed to remain on kufr, but polytheist Arabs were given only Islam or the sword. For conquered lands, if the land is divided, it becomes owned by Muslims and thus 'ushri; if left with the original cultivators, kharaj is imposed.
This reasoning is derived from the sunnah of the Prophet and the actions of the companions.
5
Apply the classification
Given a specific land, determine its status by checking: is it in the Arabian Peninsula? If yes, 'ushri. If not, was it conquered by force and not divided? If yes, kharaji. If conquered and divided, or inhabitants embraced Islam, 'ushri. If a peace treaty, kharaji.

Chapter of 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya.
Chapter of 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya.
The land of the Arabs, and what its inhabitants embraced Islam, or was conquered by force and divided among the plunderers, is 'ushri. And the Sawad...
That is: the land which. That is: by force and domination.
Chapter of 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya [i.e., explaining their rulings and their expenditures. (T, Ayni)] An explanation of what is taken from the dhimmi after explaining what makes one a dhimmi. He mentioned 'ushr to complete the financial obligations, and he gave it precedence; because it carries a meaning of worship so it is spent as zakat is spent, and intention is required in it. It would be appropriate to say: chapter of kharaj; because it explains the obligation upon the dhimmi. Kharaj is a name for what comes out of the produce of land, then it was used metaphorically for what the sultan takes, by way of metonymy, so it is said: So-and-so paid the kharaj of his land, and the people of the land paid the kharaj of their heads meaning jizya. Jizya is a name for the wealth taken from the dhimmi. 'Ushr is what is taken from the produce of land at one-tenth. (Fath)
Land of the Arabs [it is what is beyond the rural area of Iraq to the farthest Hajar in Yemen lengthwise, and from Jeddah and its adjacent coast to the border of Syria widthwise. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/434)] It is what is between al-Udhaib to the farthest Yemen in length, and from the sand of Yabrin (near Aleppo) to the end of Samawah between Kufa and Syria, and it is the land of Hijaz, Tihamah, Yemen, Mecca, Taif, and the desert. The Arabian Peninsula its lengthwise boundary is from Aden to the rural area of Iraq, and its width to the land of Syria. (Fath) 'And what its inhabitants embraced Islam' is attached to 'the land of the Arabs', meaning: every land whose inhabitants embraced Islam or was conquered by force (with fat-h, but the jurists deviate from correct usage and pronounce it with dammah on the 'ayn; it is from homonyms, used for obedience and coercion, and the latter is intended here), i.e., was conquered by force and divided among the plunderers, is 'ushri. As for the land of the Arabs being entirely 'ushri: because the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Rightly Guided Caliphs did not take kharaj from the Arabs, and because kharaj is like fay', so it is not established on their land just as it is not established on their persons. This is because the condition of imposing kharaj is that the inhabitants are permitted to remain on kufr, as in the Sawad of Iraq. But the polytheist Arabs accept only Islam or the sword, so the condition is not met. Similarly, every land whose inhabitants embraced Islam or was conquered by force and divided among the plunderers is also 'ushri; because the need for initiating taxation on a Muslim, 'ushr is more fitting for him; due to it containing meaning of worship, and because it is lighter as it pertains only to the produce itself, so it is more appropriate for the Muslim's condition. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'Its inhabitants willingly' and the pronoun is masculine considering the word. (T, Ayni) 'And the Sawad etc.' is the land of Iraq, so named for the greenness of its trees and crops. (Ayni) It is a subject with its conjuncts, and the predicate is his statement: 'kharajiya', meaning the land of Iraq and every land conquered by force and not divided among the plunderers, and likewise every land whose inhabitants the Imam made peace with, is kharaji. As for the Sawad, because 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) when he conquered the Sawad imposed kharaj upon them in the presence of the Companions, and he imposed it upon Egypt when 'Amr ibn al-'As conquered it in the year twenty of Hijra, and the Companions agreed upon imposing kharaj upon Syria when 'Umar conquered Jerusalem, and all the cities of Syria were conquered by peace treaties but their lands were conquered by force under Yazid ibn Abi Sufyan.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on 'Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya, which are financial obligations imposed on land and non-Muslim subjects. The author first defines these terms: 'Ushr is a one-tenth tax on agricultural produce of Muslim lands, treated like zakat (intention required, spent similarly). Kharaj is a land tax on non-Muslims (or lands held by them), and Jizya is the poll tax on dhimmis. The passage then classifies lands into two types: 'ushri lands and kharaji lands. 'Ushri lands include: (1) the lands of the Arabian Peninsula (because the Prophet and Caliphs never imposed kharaj there, and the condition for kharaj—that inhabitants remain in kufr—is absent since polytheist Arabs were given only Islam or death); (2) any land whose inhabitants embraced Islam voluntarily; (3) any land conquered by force and then divided among the Muslim soldiers (ghanimah). All these become 'ushri. Kharaji lands include: (1) the Sawad (Iraq) and any land conquered by force but not divided (left in the hands of the original inhabitants with kharaj); (2) any land where the Imam makes a peace treaty with the inhabitants (sulhan) agreeing to kharaj. Examples: 'Umar's imposition on Sawad and Egypt, and the Companions' consensus on Syria. The reason is that kharaj is like fay' (booty for the community), and it is not taken from Muslim individuals or their land.

Key Terms
عشر ('ushr): A tax of one-tenth on agricultural produce of 'ushri lands (Muslim-owned or conquered and divided).
خراج (kharaj): A land tax imposed on kharaji lands (non-Muslim owned or conquered lands not divided) based on the land's productivity.
جزية (jizya): A poll tax on free adult male dhimmis in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
أرض العرب (ard al-arab): The Arabian Peninsula, defined geographically: from al-'Udhaib to Yemen lengthwise and from Yabrin to Samawah widthwise.
سواد (sawad): The fertile agricultural region of Iraq, so named for its greenery; it is kharaji land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for land to be classified as 'ushri according to the passage?
A) The land is in the Arabian Peninsula.
B) The inhabitants embraced Islam.
C) The land was conquered by force and not divided.
D) The land was conquered by force and divided among the soldiers.

Key Takeaway
Land classification into 'ushri or kharaji depends on its location, the status of its inhabitants at conquest, and whether it was divided as spoils; this determines which tax applies.

Kharaji Lands: Classification and Proof
Explains which lands are subject to kharaj and why

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which lands are classified as kharajiyya according to the Hanafi school and explain the basis of that classification.

Core Idea
Kharaj is imposed on lands conquered by force and not divided, except Makkah; the land remains owned by its inhabitants.

This passage clarifies which conquered lands are subject to kharaj. It establishes that al-Sawad (Iraq) is the prototype, and any land conquered by force and left undivided, or settled by peace treaty, shares this status. Makkah is a notable exception. The ruling is based on the consensus of the Companions, and the passage also addresses internal and external disputes about specific lands and ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What is the ruling about? The passage states that certain lands are 'kharajiyya' – subject to kharaj. Note the conditions: conquered by force and not divided, or settled by peace treaty.
2
Locate the proof
What evidence is cited? The consensus (ijma) of the Companions, specifically the actions of Umar and the Companions on al-Sawad, Syria, and Egypt. Also, the practice of Umar in al-Sawad is presented as the basis.
The text says: 'because Umar... imposed kharaj in the presence of the Companions.'
3
Understand the exception
Makkah is exempted from kharaj despite being conquered by force. Why? Because the Prophet left it for its people without imposing kharaj, out of reverence for the sacred city.
Exception proves the rule.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held that Basra is kharajiyya, but the text says Basra is 'ushri by consensus of the Companions. The majority view (mufta bihi) is that Basra is ushri.
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, individual ijtihad can differ, but the relied-upon view follows the consensus.
5
Recognize the inter-madhhab dispute
The Hanafi school holds that the inhabitants own the land. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one narration) hold that the land is waqf for Muslims and the inhabitants are tenants. The Hanafi view is supported by Abu Bakr al-Razi's ten-fold refutation.
The practical difference: in Hanafi, the landowner can sell; in Shafi'i, the land is not transferable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني)] مبتدأ مع معطوفاته، والخبر قوله: "خراجية" أي أرض العراق وكل أرض فتحت غلبة لم تقسم بين الغانمين وكذلك كل أرض صالح الإمام أهلها خراجية، أما السواد فلان عمر الله حين فتح السواد ووضع عليهم الخراج بمحضر من الصحابة له، ووضع على مصر حين فتحها عمرو بن العاص له سنة عشرين من الهجرة، واجتمعت الصحابة على وضع الخراج على الشام حين افتتح: عمر الله بيت المقدس، ومدن الشام كلها فتحت صلحاً وأراضيها فتحت عنوة على يزيد بن أبي سفيان، وما فتح عنوةً وأقرّ أهله عليه أو صالحهم خراجية، واستثنى من الأراضي المفتوحة عنوة مكة شرفها الله تعالى؛ لأنه عليه افتتحها عنوة وتركها لأهلها ولم يوظف عليها الخراج، وقوله: "وأقر أهله عليه ليس بشرط في كون الأرض خراجية إنما الشرط عدم قسمتها، أما كون هذه الأراضي خراجية؛ فلأن الحاجة إلى ابتداء التوظيف على الكافر، والخراج أليق به لما فيه من معنى العقوبة؛ لأنه يشبه الجزية التي هي عقوبة على الكافر؛ ولأن في الخراج تغليظاً ولهذا يجبر عليه وإن لم يزرع؛ لأنه يتعلق بعين الأرض، وأما العشر فمتعلق بالخارج، ثم أرض السواد مملوكة لأهلها عندنا، وقال الشافعي: ليست بمملوكة لهم، وإنما هو وقف على المسلمين وأهلها مستأجرون وبه قال مالك وأحمد في رواية وقد ردّ هذا أبو بكر الرازي في كتابه "الأحكام من عشرة وجوه. [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٥/١] (فتح)
عشرية لإجماع الصحابة على ذلك، والقياس أن تكون خراجية؛ لأنها افتتحت عنوة، وأقر أهلها عليها وهي من جملة أراضي العراق، ولكن ترك القياس بإجماعهم كما خرج عن القياس مكة تعظيما لها، وهذا عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: إن البصرة خراجية كما هو القياس [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٥/١] (معدن، فتح) جريب: [ وهو ستون ذراعاً بذراع الكسرى، وهو سبع قبضات ] أي مما يزرع تلك الأرض؛ لأن المؤون متفاوتة فيجب على أخفها الأكثر، وعلى أكثرها الأقل، وعلى الوسط الوسط، والكرم أخفها؛ لأنه يبقى دهراً مديداً، والزرع أكثرها مؤنة لاحتياجه إلى الكراب وإلقاء البذر والحصاد والدياس ونحو ذلك في كل سنة، والرطبة بين الأخف والأكثر؛ لأنه لا يحتاج إلى البذر كل عام وتدوم =
And al-Sawad etc.: it is the land of Iraq, named for the greenness of its trees and crops. (Ayni) The subject and its conjuncts, the predicate is "kharajiyya": i.e., the land of Iraq and every land conquered by force not divided among the spoilers, and every land whose people the Imam made peace are kharajiyya. As for al-Sawad, because Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) when he conquered al-Sawad imposed kharaj on them in the presence of the Companions... And what was conquered by force and its people were left on it or he made peace with them is kharajiyya. Makkah is excepted... As for these lands being kharajiyya, because the need for initial imposition is on the disbeliever, and kharaj is more suitable for him due to the element of punishment... Then the land of al-Sawad is owned by its people according to us, but al-Shafi'i said it is not owned by them... One-tenth (of Basra) is ushri by consensus of the Companions, otherwise analogy would make it kharajiyya... And Abu Yusuf said Basra is kharajiyya as per analogy. Jareeb: it is sixty cubits by the cubit of Kisra...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses kharaji lands. It begins with al-Sawad (Iraq) as the primary example of kharaji land, established by Umar with consensus of the Companions. It then rules that any land conquered by force and not divided among the spoilers, or land settled by peace treaty, is kharaji. Makkah is an exception, conquered by force but left for its people without kharaj due to its sanctity. The rationale is that kharaj is a punishment for disbelief, so it is imposed on disbelievers. The passage also addresses a dispute with al-Shafi'i over ownership of kharaji lands; Hanafis hold that the land remains owned by its inhabitants. Another issue is that Basra is ushri by consensus, though analogy would suggest it should be kharaji; Abu Yusuf held it is kharaji. The measurement jareeb is defined for assessing kharaj on different crops.

Key Terms
خراجية (kharajiyya): Lands subject to kharaj (a land tax on non-Muslims).
سواد (sawad): The fertile region of Iraq, specifically the land conquered by Umar.
جريب (jarib): A unit of area (60 cubits by 60 cubits) used for measuring land for kharaj assessment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following lands is an exception to the rule that conquered lands are kharaji?
A) Al-Sawad
B) Basra
C) Makkah
D) Egypt

Key Takeaway
Kharaji lands are those conquered by force and not divided, or settled by peace treaty, with Makkah as an exception; the Hanafi school holds that ownership remains with the inhabitants.

Reviving Dead Lands: Ushri or Kharaji? The Criterion of Proximity vs. Irrigation
Explains the criterion for ushri/kharaji of revived dead land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the criteria for classifying revived dead land as ushri or kharaji according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and apply these criteria to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The tax status of revived dead land hinges on either proximity to existing land or its source of irrigation.

When a Muslim revives a piece of dead land (mawat), the question arises: should it be treated as ushri land (subject to ushr) or kharaji land (subject to kharaj)? The Hanafi school has two views: one based on the land's proximity to already classified land, and the other based on how the land is irrigated. This lesson unpacks both positions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the contested ruling
The act is reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat). The ruling in question is whether the revived land becomes ushri or kharaji. This determines the tax obligation on its produce or rent.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's criterion: proximity
Abu Yusuf holds that the revived land takes the same classification as the nearest inhabited land. If closer to ushri land, it is ushri; if closer to kharaji land, it is kharaji. His proof is the principle that 'a part of a thing takes its ruling' (juz' al-shay' yu'ta lahu hukmuhu).
This is based on qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Understand Muhammad's criterion: irrigation method
Muhammad al-Shaybani considers what the land is revived with. If revived by natural sources (rainwater, great rivers, or wells that no one owns), it is ushri. If revived by a man-made canal (like a king's canal), it is kharaji. He argues that water is the essence of life and cultivation, so the method of irrigation is more directly relevant than mere proximity.
4
Note the exception for a non-Muslim
A non-Muslim who revives dead land must pay kharaj regardless of proximity or irrigation method. This is because kharaj is a land tax that applies to non-Muslims as a sign of their protected status (dhimma).
Even if the land is in ushri territory or irrigated by ushri water.
5
Recognize the practical difference and mufta bihi
The practical consequence appears when the land is equidistant from both ushri and kharaji areas, or when a Muslim revives land with a dug canal. According to Abu Yusuf the equidistant land becomes ushri (as stated in the text); according to Muhammad it becomes kharaji if irrigated by a canal. Later Hanafi works often adopt Muhammad's view.
The text mentions that if equidistant, it is ushri (by implication from Abu Yusuf's view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أحيي إلخ [ أي لو أصلح رجل وزرع أرضاً مواتاً. (مستخلص)] بالبناء للمفعول و"موات" نائب الفاعل، وفي بعض النسخ بالبناء للفاعل وبزيادة "أرض" في المتن أي لو أحيي أرض موات، أي أصلحها وزرعها، والموات: الأرض التي تعذر زرعها وهي غير مملوكة بعيدة عن العامر، وعند محمد والثلاثة: يعتبر عدم الارتفاق لا البعد. وقوله: "يعتبر قربه وهذا حكمها، فإن كانت إلى الخراج أقرب، فهي خراجية، وإن كانت إلى العشر أقرب فهي عشرية وهذا عند أبي يوسف؛ لأن جزء الشيء يعطى له حكمه، واعتبره محمد بما يحيى به، فإن كان أو عين مستخرجة أو بالأنهار العظام التي لا يملكها أحد كان عشرياً، وكذا إذا أحياها بماء السماء؛ النماء والحياة هو الماء فكان اعتباره أولى وعلى هذا إن كان أحياها بنهر محتفر كنهر الملك ونهر كانت خراجية، وهذا التفصيل في حق المسلم، وأما الكافر فيجب عليه الخراج، وإن كان في حيز أرض العشر أو أحياها بماء العشر. [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٥/١] (فتح) موات هي أرض تعذر زرعها لانقطاع ماء أو لغلبته عليها غير مملوكة (عيني) قربه أي قرب ما أحياه، فإن كانت بقرب الخراجي فهي خراجية وإن كانت بقرب العشري فهو عشرية، وإن كانت بين الخراجي والعشرية فهي عشرية. (مستخلص)
And if he revives dead land... [if a man improves and cultivates dead land]. The word is in passive voice, 'dead' is the subject. In some copies it is active with 'land' added. Dead land is land whose cultivation has become difficult, unowned, and far from inhabited land. According to Muhammad and the three, the criterion is lack of use, not distance. And his saying: 'its closeness is considered' is the ruling: if it is closer to kharaji land, it is kharaji; if closer to ushri, it is ushri. This is according to Abu Yusuf, because a part of a thing takes its ruling. Muhammad considered what it is revived with: if by a well or great rivers that no one owns, it is ushri; similarly if revived by rainwater. But if by a dug canal like the king's canal, it is kharaji. This detail applies to a Muslim; as for a disbeliever, kharaj is wajib upon him even if in ushri territory or revived by ushri water.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage deals with the revival of dead land (ihya al-mawat). It defines dead land as land that cannot be cultivated, is unowned, and is far from inhabited areas. There is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: Abu Yusuf considers proximity to existing land—if revived land is closer to kharaji land, it is kharaji; if closer to ushri, it is ushri. Muhammad considers the source of irrigation: if revived by rainwater or great unowned rivers, it is ushri; if by a man-made canal, it is kharaji. For a non-Muslim, kharaj is always wajib regardless of location or water source. The commentary also notes that if the dead land is equally distant from both, it becomes ushri.

Key Terms
موات (mawat): Dead land: unowned, non-cultivable land far from inhabited areas.
إحياء (ihya): Revival of dead land by cultivation or construction.
عشر (ushr): Tithe: 10% or 5% of agricultural produce paid on ushri land.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax: variable tax on kharaji land, often heavier than ushr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a Muslim revives dead land using a canal he dug, what is the classification according to Muhammad al-Shaybani?
A) Ushri, because it is revived land.
B) Kharaji, because it is man-made irrigation.
C) It depends on the nearest inhabited land.
D) Both A and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
The classification of revived dead land as ushri or kharaji is determined by either proximity to existing land (Abu Yusuf) or the method of irrigation (Muhammad), with a non-Muslim always paying kharaj.
